A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash va ue of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these po’icies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments —more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

TO BE LEFT UNTIL CALLED FOR
“On Saturday morning Sir samuel Griffith, the Premier of Queensland, had a long interview with Mr. G.R. Dibbs at the Colonial Seeretary's office Upon the Federation question Sir
Samuel Griffith said has faith in the present Goverment adhering to its promise, therefore with satisfaction he leaves the subject to the care of Mr. Dibbs.”-Daily paper.
THE MAN IN CHREE: “Oh, yes, you can leave it, but I can't be responible for it. My predecessou has left the place in such a state-"

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 6, 1892.
This Ungrateful Nation.
Several months ago Charles Lilley, Chief
Justice of Queensland, began to assume in
public a certain ultra-democratic attitude,
which created a considerable sensation,
and caused the Cabinet of the Imperial-
istic Samuel Griffith to breathe forth
threats of vengeance. At the time when
Judge Lilley made his first bid for the
leadership of the Republican party in Aus-
tralia, the country was troubled by various
small wage-problems, which were then com-
monly designated as the “shearers’ insurrec-
tion,' 1 and it was held in many quarters that
Lilley’, s disbelief in royal institutions would
intensify the trouble, and incite the frenzied
bush man to demand more pay. That class
of fatted individuals commonly designated
in the papers “all right-thinking persons”
even complained that the Queensland Chief
Justice (like the Republican Chief Justice
of Victoria), was inciting to anarchy and
civil war, and it was generally under-
stood that his language would cause a pain-
ful sensation in England. This latter
prophecy, at all events, has come true ; Sir
Charles’ views upon Australian independ-
ence have at last dawned, in their full horror
and malignity, on the British intellect, and
Britain is at once sorrowful and indignant.
We have before us some six or eight extracts
from various English papers all dealing with
the subject from the same standpoint, and
of these assorted Avails the following, from
the Home N lavs, is a fair example :
His comments contain the essentials of the case for an
Independent Australia. It cannot hut be regarded as a
curious fact that men such as the Chief Justice of Queens-
land should adopt this attitude. They have seen Aus-
tralia grow great and prosperous under the British fhi"-;
they know how much the mother country lias sacrificed
for the sake of her colonies, and how in the last half
century she has given them, with most generous hand, all
they ask, and in return they promise her separation and
practical isolation. In Imperial, as in other matters,
virtue is to be its own reward, according to the views of
Sir Charles Lilley and those who think with him.
Magnanimous as the mother country has been in the past,
he naturally hopes that she will carry her good nature a
little further and permit the colonies to secede just when
and how they please. It is not surprising that such an
observer should view the Federal Constitution Bill with
disapproval. It is too patriotic, too loyal. Sir Charles
Lilley claims to represent “the real opinion of Aus-
tralia." But for the appearance of his name one might
confound his views with those of the fleetest of globe-
trotters, or of a fifth-rate member of some colonial
Legislature. If Sir Charles Lilley’s opinion is that of
Australia, for whom do men like Sir Henry Parses and
others responsible for the drafting of the Constitution
Bill speak ? For themselves? If so, their lease of popu-
larity and power needs explanation.
# # #
The other complaints are so exactly
similar in their tone as not to be worth re-
producing ; the one paper speaks for all (he
rest. And it also speaks for the whole
British nation, for whenever Australia
threatens to sever the “silken bonds” of
empire all Great Britain strikes a paralytic
attitude of pained surprise at the black aud
fathomless baseness of “ our kawlinies.”
The inevitable assumption on which the
incarnate Bull always travels is that, in some
unspecified way, and for some reason that is
never defined, Australia owes to Britain a
debt of gratitude which long ages of devotion
will be insufficient to repay. Therefore the
Home News and its contemporaries, as the
personification of Bull and all his works,
rise to reproach us with the things —not
particularised—which “the mother country
has sacrificed for the sake of her colonies, ’
and the concessions—not further explained—
which “ she has given them with most
generous hand." Even the British journa-
list cannot remember what these mythical
concessions and sacrifices are, for the suffi-
cient reason that they never existed. The
whole theory is a dull, dismal, bombastic
lie, a pompous perjury, the most hollow of
all hollow pretences. J. Bull, C.M.Gr.,
never made any “sacrifices;” but it is the
nature of the pachydermatous animal to
assume that he is a blessing to all with whom
he comes in contact, and that everybody
whom he ever exploited in trade or war
owes him a debt of gratitude. Therefore,
he is surprised at the thanklessness of the
Chi naman whom he forced to buy his opium,
and the Irishman whose country he has
depopulated, and the Burmese whose land
he has filled with blood and ashes ; and the
soulless insensibility of everybody he has ever
insulted or plundered fills his soul with pain
England is the Pecksniff among humanity
- —the one great and enduring hypocrite of
the earth—and when it strikes an attitude
as the weeping parent of many thankless
nations, it is a fearsome spectacle to behold.
# # #
As we have already mentioned, the spread-
eagle Englishman, when he lodges his
perennial complaint concerning the malign
attitude of Australia, never descends to
particulars. Now and then, however, he
comes perilously near doing so, and on these
occasions the cause of his woe generally
seems to come under six separate and equally
tearful headings ; (1) England has defended
and still defends Australia from foreign aggres-
sion ; (2) England has supplied and still
supplies Australia with vast sums of money
by way of loans; (3) Australia was “founded”
by Englishmen, and, therefore, owes a debt of
gratitude to England as the original source
of its existence ; (4) Australia has grown
•great and prosperous under the British flag ;
(5) Australia, apart from all' pecuniary
considerations, stands to England in the
relation of a son to a father, and is
bound to that parent by a common
origin and a common speech ; (6) Eng-
land has made many “concessions’ to
Australia in the shape of local self-govern-
ment and free political institutions, which
same c mcessions should be remembered
with profound thankfulness for all time.
These, in a general way, are the alleged
obligations which this country owes to the
little, sea-girt, fog-haunted isle in the far
North Sea, and the disregard of them by the
Australian nation makes the Briton feel
that he has thawed a frozen serpent in his
bosom to his own undoing.
* * *
Possibly the Englishman—the dull, clod-
like Bull of the Nineteenth Century—
solemnly believes in the reality of Ids six
grievances. By much weary iteration he
may even have come to believe that, in some
shadowy and uncertain way, ho has defended
Australia, at some time or other, against
somebody, and spent much blood and
treasure in doing so. Yet the theory is (lie
most conspicuous lie that ever glared forth
in the pages of fiction. Australia never was
attacked—therefore, it was never defended
against anybody. England never spent a
single man or a single coin in protecting it
from any outside enemies, for it never had
any. It is claimed, of course, that this is a
mere matter of accident ; that if it had been
visited by enemies in the past England would
have protected it, and that if it should be
visited by enemies in the future the mighty
arms of the mother-land will be its defence ;
but this is mere theory at the best, and
nobody caii well be grateful for the things
that may be or for the things that might have
been. Ic is altogether too dubious and too
shadowy. Australia cannot well raise a sen-
timent of undying thankfulness because it
might have been attacked by some of Eng-
land’s enemies, and if so it might have been
defended by the resources of the Empire.
It is as reasonable to ask that a man should
repay with interest the money that his friend
might have lent him, but didn’t—that he
should cherish all his life the memory of the
hero who might have saved his life if the
occasion had arisen, but who was prevented
from doing it because the occasion didn’t
arise—or that he shotild put up a tombstone
over the brother who 'might have died, but
who, by chance, has remained alive. Life is
too short for calculating such elaborate
possibilities ; therefore, the world prefers to
deal with facts, and the hard fact in this case
is that England has never yet defended Aus-
tralia on any occasion whatsoever. Even
the British fleet which roams Australian
waters is only there for the defence of British
shipping ; there is a similar fleet off the South
American coast for exactly the same purpose,
and Australia is under just the same obliga-
tion for that squadron’s presence as aie
Brazil and Argentina for the presence of the
other British squadron by which their shores
are haunted.
* # #
As regards the second count in the indict-
ment, Australia has assuredly Obtained vast
sums of money from England, and for these
this country owes a debt of gratitude that
is to say, it owes exactly as much gratitude
for the principal as England owes for (ho
interest, and so far the dull and thankless
Eull has shown no sign of being at all
grateful for the interest, which has always
been promptly paid. It owes as much
gratitude and worship and adoration as any
solvent debtor does to his creditor —as much
as Rothschild or Vandekbilt does to the
man who holds his bill in the ordinary
course of business—as much as the Bank of
England does to any grocer or bootmaker
who chances to have one of its notes in his
pocket—and just as much as the patriotic
editor of the Home News does to the lady
who has washed his collar and his shirt, and
whose account is not yet settled. In other
words, it is a mere matter of business, and
if England wants gratitude it must do with-
out interest, or if it wants interest it must
do without the gratitude, for it can't have
gratitude and interest both. And the plea
that Australia was “founded” by Englishmen,
and is therefore under an eternal obligation
to England for founding it, may also be dis-
missed in a sentence. Australia is grateful,
despite certain unpleasant features in their
characters, to these Englishmen who estab-
lished the first beginnings of a new nation
in the Pacific seas. But these English-
men and their sons are now the Austra-
lian nation ; the blatant John Bull who
demands to be held in reverence as the
origin of Australia’s greatness is the Bull
who stayed at home and wasn’t the origin of
anything, and the person from whom ho
demands the reverence is the one who did
the work for which that reverence is to be
paid. Such unspeakable gall as this has no
duplicate in all history. The Englishmen
who built the cities of Australia, and con-
quered the mysteries of the great drought-
land of the new continent, and who are now
the new Australian people, are required to
be grateful for their own work to the men
who never saw Australia and many of whom
never will see it to the end of time.
* * #
The fourth item in the category —one on
which the Home News lays special import-
ance—may be admitted without further
question. Australia has grown great and
prosperous under the British flag. ‘ That flag
is a piece of cloth of various colours, and it
has been fastened to the end of a stick,
and the stick has been stood up on its
end by accident on Australian soil. What
it has had to do witli the prosperity
the writer docs not state. Barrb g
the colour there is very little difference
between the flag and a clean shirt on a line,
and as there is more clean shirt than flag it
is equally appropriate to state that Australia
lias prospered under the benign influence of
the under-garment as that it lias been
materially assisted by the gaudy banner of
Britain. Moreover, the flag is sometimes up
and sometimes uovvn, and the country gels
on just as well when it is down as when it is
up ; and in any case a flag really belongs to
whoever pays for the material and the stick ;
and further, Australia never asked for or
wanted the flag ; and last of all, the whole
subject is too miserably puerile to bo worth
attention. The fact that (he Australians
are, in the main, of British origin, and are
linked to Britain by a common descent and a
common language, is of equally small account
when put forward as a reason why Australia
should remain for ever faithful to the
Empire and for ever loyal to Britain. The
Britons of to-day made neither the language
nor the origin ; they received them both by
accident, and they passed them both on
because they couldn’t help it. Every nation
ha.s an origin, and every nation, so far as is
known, has a language, but England is the
only one of them all that is so far gone in
the horrible stupidity of its dotage as to
fancy that the world owes it an eternal debt
for possessing two things that everybody
owns and nobody can get rid of.
* # #
Even the fact that Britain has made many
alleged “ concessions ” to Australia does not
move us to gratitude somehow. Possibly
we are by nature an ungrateful race.
England has “conceded ” the right to make
our own laws within certain limits ; it would
have been an act of brutal tyranny to have
refused that right, and we are not necessarily
thankful to England for not being more of a
tyrant than it is. When a small man meets
a large one on a lonely street he is not bound
to worship that large man for ever after-
wards because he doesn’t jump on him by
virtue of his size and strength, and the two
situations are exactly parallel. Australia, at
the time it first claimed the right of self-
government, was certainly too weak to
enforce its claims by war, but the demand
was as justifiable as if it had been backed by
100,000 armed men. And when the Home
News asks, in accents of wild surprise, if w'e
expect that England will permit us to secede
just when and how we please, the answer
comes promptly in two words ; We do. The
t ime is gone by when one nation can belong
as a matter of right to another nation, or
w hen one man can belong to another man.
I he great principle of the British constitu-
tion is (hat the people are the last court of
appeal, and if the British people are the last
court of appeal in Britain, then the Aus-
tralian people are of the same effect in
Australia. The Englishman, it is true,
regards this rule. as applying to him-
self only ; when he utters the well-worn
axiom that the voice - of the people is the
■ voice of (Jon he means really that the voice
of the English people is the utterance of (he
(ion of the whole universe ; but then this
is a purely English view of the matter, and
(lie incarnate Bull has this opinion all to
himself. By virtue of the noble British
Constitution, 1 he principles of human liberty,
and the common rights of humanity, these
provinces expect that England will “allow
them to secede” peacefully, unquestioningly
and just when a id how they think best!
They owe nothing to the Empire either
in money or gratitude ; they have re-
ceived nothing from the Empire that has
nut been paid for in full ; they have built
their own cities, and opened up their own
country, and organised their own Govern-
ment just as England did, and they expect,
just as England does, to control the works
they have created at their own absolute plea-
sure. They have lived under the shadow of
the British flag and they will send that flag
back undamaged ; they have enjoyed the in°
estimable benefits of the British language,
and they will leave that language in as good
repair as they found it. ThcTconnection ends
fheie ; it is a purely business relationship
though disfigured by much irrelevant brass-
band and national anthem, and when one
member refuses, some day or other, to renew
the partnership, it will dissolve in peace. At
any rate, we hope so.
A Matter of “Crood Taste.”
Near the close of the Nineteenth Century a
number of men, amongst whom were several
Republicans, met as representatives of modern
democracy in an alleged free and enlightened
State, to pass a vote of condolence to the parents
of a young man who had just died, a young man
with whom they were absolutely unacquainted,
whoso only claim upon their attention was the
lact that, had ho lived long enough, he might
one day have been crowned king of Great
Britain. Of this young man they knew
little, and of that little nothing strikingly
to his credit. He had achieved nothing for
humanity in even themeagrest measure, and he
gave every evidential symptom of achieving less
if he had lived long enough to achieve anything.
Yet, at the end of a period of time marked by
wonderful mental and material progress
in the world’s history, the Parliament of
New South Wales, headed by a Premier
who claims to be a Republican himself, passed,
with one single exception, a lying and hypo-
critical \ ote of condolence —mnply because the
deceased uas a Prince of the Blood Royal, and
for no other earthly reason. One man, and
only one man, had the courage and the honesty
to dissent from this piece of purely official—not
even sentimental—humbug. In a temperate
ami dignified little spieech, Mr. Arthur Rak.
the Labour member for the Murrumbidgee,
stated his powerlessness, as a Republican, to
join with the other members in condoling with
the griefs of Royalty—simply on the ground
that it teas Royalty. His remarks on the subject
were destitute of any single grain of offence or
vulgar aspersion, and only the most pronounced
toady could take umbrage at them.
* * #
The other day an Africander editor was
hooted and assaulted by a mob of Royalists
because he took occasion to print in his paper
something of the lately-dead Duke other
than laudatory ; and apparently there are
not wanting in New South Wales per
sons who are ambitious of dragging the
province down to the same level. Professor
Wallace remarks, in his book on Russia, that
freedom of speech is perfect in that country,
provided no one discusses either politics or
religion, and did the Australian daily press
really represent the feelings of the masses and
the native-born and younger generation, instead
of those of the British-born and propertied
classes who, up to now, have controlled the
country, it would be plain that there is
little to choose in point of sentiment be-
tween Australia and the Transvaal. For
no sooner had Mr. Rae made his speech
than an assumedly democratic evening paper
made a savage assault upon him, and the
upper-crust circle of polite society in Wagga
convened a public meeting requesting him to
resign his seat in Parliament, the Mayor, an
approved loyalist and a lavish entertainer of
vice-regality, sending a personal telegram to
the Governor expressive of his abhorrence of
Republicanism in general and Mr. Rae
in particular. The Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, an alleged democratic morning news-
paper, attacks Mr. Rae on the grounds of
“good taste,” as though political institutions
and conventional etiquette were synonymous
terms, and government were conducted after the
fashion of drawing-room entertainments. The
Republicans who lopped off the head of
Charles 1. were monsters of bad taste, and
the French Revolutionists who performed the
same kind ollice for Louts XVI. were impolite
to the last degree. Every inch of the ground
gained in the path of progress is a lapse in
decorum, and bad manners crowd the pages of
history. Mr. Rae acted according to his con-
victions, ho assailed the private character of no
one, ami, compared with the hypocritical com-
promise of many avowed Republicans in the
New South Wales Assembly, his position is
uniquely straightforward and honest. These
pseudo-democrats thought with him, but they
were too cowardly to face the great mob of
decorously conventional people whose minds
run in the rut of effete usage; they feared to be
accused of acting in “ bad taste.” ”
* * #
Whenever a royal grant is mooted in the
English Parliament, Laeouchere seizes the
occasion to make a Republican protest against
further pensioning the Royal Family, and no one
now thinks of accusing him of “ bad taste ” or
lack of sympathy. Rae simply followed
Laroughere’s example. On the grounds of
sympathy with parents who had lost a son,
with a young girl whose prospective husband
had died, he was entirely with the House and
said so. His contention, however* an eminently
just one, was that such were not the grounds
actuating the members of the New South Wales


Parliament. He rightly asserted that no
Ba „ e of condolence, no official declaration of
U ~',u ) alhy, was ever sent to the widows or
;[ ( | r en of men to whom humanity owed some-
and that the simulated departmental
,r ief of a democratic community was never put
on tap save over a royal or an imperial corpse.
, I now, after centuries of fighting for the
-io-lit of freedom of speech, after hundreds of
boastful assertions regarding the emancipation
f ie human intellect, after a liberty of
discussion of political, social, and religious
übiects, to attain which Russia is yearly
wasting tons of dynamite and hundreds of
human lives, the plucky little man from the
Murrumbidgee is (so far as the will of the
ess goes) to be ousted from his seat in
* democratic Parliament, simply because he
] uW l the honesty to challenge the integrity of
ihe sympathy so loudly expressed by a chamber
of supposed Republicans and semi-Socialists.
# * *
However, the press criticism of Mr. Raf.’s
conduct will not hurt him very much. The
public meetings in which he has been burned
in verbal effigy need cause him no alarm. These
meetings were convened by the people whose
votes were never recorded on behalf of Repub-
lican Rah. They are just about as representa-
tive of the feeling of the electorate as the cele-
brated ball-cartridge meeting at the old Ex-
hibition Building, on the occasion of Queen
Victoria's Jubilee, was representative of
the sentiments of the people of Sydney at
that time. Arthur Rah was sent to Par-
liament by the shearers and other workers to
represent Labour, and all the while he repre-
sents Labour faithfully, Labour will stand by
him. The mere vapourings of the uppercut
section of the community, whose very existence
depends upon slavishly ministering to a con-
ventional order, and whose very breath of life
is the air of fashion, he can afford to treat with
indifference. He is not and never has been
their representative, and he would be guilty of
an act of the supremest folly if he obeyed their
mandate to resign his seat. It would, moreover,
lie an act of treachery to the workers who sent
him to Parliament to watch over their interests,
and Mr. Rah can serve Labour in many worse
wavs than by his bold avowal of the Republican
principles to which he has been so true.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Identification.
fx Victoria, a week or two ago, a certain Mrs.
Marendaz lodged a complaint that she had
been criminally assaulted by some man un-
known. Thereupon the police arrested a swags-
mau named Wilson', and the prosecutrix came
along to “identify” him. The first attempt
was a failure; Mrs. Marendaz couldn’t posi-
tively remember having seen Wilson at any
time in her whole life before. He was still
detained in custody, however, and on a second
visit the lady was still unable to recall his face,
though it began to be a little more familiar.
The third day she commenced to recollect him,
and at the fourth or fifth attempt she remem-
bered him distinctly, and he was thereupon
committed for trial. When the case
comes up for hearing the bald fact
will be placed before the jury that the
prosecutrix “identified” Wilson, and the
method of identification will probably never be
fully explained. The chances are, in this
instance, that Mrs. Markndaz fully and
honestly believes she does recollect all about
the prisoner, and at t he same time the odds are
forty to one that she is utterly mistaken. The
fact that she was unable to recognise him on
the first attempt is a solid evidence that, up to
that date, she had never seen him. She did
remember him at a later attempt, but that does
not necessarily prove that she remembered him
as the man who committed the assault —she
simply recollected him as the man whom site
had seen during sundry previous attempts at
identification, and the recollections got mixed.
In such a case the witness must either identify
the prisoner light off or else her evidence is
absolutely valueless ; in other words, she must
remember by a natural process—not simply
learn to do so by an artificial method. Any-
body can learn, honestly enough, to recollect a
thing which never happened, simply by dwel-
ling upon it with sufficient persistency. In
Walter Besant’s novel, “ The Bell of St.
Paul ’s,” an old, coarse, utterly matter-of-fact
usurer is confronted with a forged will to
which his name is affixed as a witness, and is
requested to swear to his signature. He has
ho suspicions of the bona-fides of the document ;
he merely concludes that he has somehow for-
gotten having signed it, and he starts with
awful persistence to remember it. Then he
h'arns that loss of memory is a sign of incipient
softening of the brain, and in his frantic resolve
to escape the softening he resolves that he will
remember that signature or burst. He gives up
hquur and takes violent exercise and ice-cold
baths, and has galvanic shocks applied to his
head and the soles of his feet, and by all these
processes he comes at last to recollect the
whole of the case—despite the fact that there
never was any case—so absolutely that he even
lends money on the bogus document. This
episode is a solemn warning to people who get
U P artificial memories by a process of hard
dunking, and identify other people on the
strength of them. Anybody, in fact, can
remember anything provided he only tries hard
enough. If we made a sufficient effort we would
probably recollect the time, during the early
struggles of The Bulletin, when Parkes came
down to the office with a blank cheque in his
hand, and told us to draw on his account for
any money that we wanted, while Abigail and
the Catholic Archbishop offered to go joint
securities for an overdraft—in fact, seeing that
tnese things didn’t happen, we would probably
remember them a great deal better than we
would if they really did happen.
The Totalisator.
I he opposition to the total isator may be said
to come from two quarters : from those con-
scientiously opposed to the legalisation of what
"'CV consider a vice ; and from bookmakers and
others, influenced by more sordid motives. We
already have examples of the absurd position
adopted by the former class. For instance,
many estimable people hold that the liquor-
traffic should be absolutely prohibited rather
than licensed. However, while drinking, like
betting, cannot be put down by Act of Parlia-
ment, few sane people will deny that, under
certain restrictions, it works less ill than if con-
ducted without supervision. On the principle
of choosing the smaller evil, betting, while it
exists, is preferable in its less noxious form. In
fact, the opponents of the totalisator very much
remind us of the anti-vaccinationists. The one
class opposes a palliative, which deprives small-
pox of its worst terrors ; the other would wink
at the operations of the welsher, the extortionate
bookmaker, and the “ crouk ” sporting
tobacconist, rather than recognise a form of
betting which, to a large extent, protects the
people from their own folly. Nor is the
opposition of the second section any more
reasonable. The totalisator does not mean the
total extinction of the bookmaker. While
the less reputable members of the craft would
be bound to go to the wall, the better class of
pencillers, freed from the competition of un-
scrupulous rivals who, never intending to pay,
can now afford to lay longer odds, must actually
benefit by its introduction. It can never be
patronised by those who make sport a business.
For instance, the owner, the trainer, the jockey,
the professional backer, and even the outsider,
in possession of the “ straight tip,” naturally
try to put their superior knowledge to the
best possible account. They depend on busi-
ness methods rather than chance, and prefer
10 to 1 from a substantial bookmaker before-
hand to waiting for the day of the race, when,
owing to a rush of public support, their “ good
thing ” may only be an even-money chance.
Again, betting on the totalisator requires the
possession of ready money and is not suitable
for those whose wagers are necessarily large.
Joe Thompson, a cheque-book, and settling
day offer to men like Mr. Donald Wallace
conveniences impossible in the case of the
machine. The latter contrivance, therefore,
cannot affect the classes already mentioned, nor
the bookmakers whom these now patronise.
With the amateur turfite, as well as the welsher,
the “ Toe-Rag Tommy,” and the “ cronk ” tote-
promoter who now victimise him, the case will
be different. Its likelihood to annihilate
these latter undesirable excrescences would
alone justify the introduction of the totali-
sator. To those people who do not make
sport a study, yet, when they visit a
racecourse for recreative purposes, like to
arouse interest by means of a wager, the
so-called “infernal machine” offers many
advantages. It relieves them at once from the
unscrupulous bookmaker, with his one-sided
terms, as well as from the welsher who
simply robs them. The money invested—less
a legitimate dividend devoted to promoting
the sport, and, perhaps, relieving distress —
returns again to the public, instead of, as now,
going to keep a horde of gaol-birds, ex-prize-
fighters, and brothel hangers-on—fellows too
lazy to work and only too cowardly to become
burglars or garrotters. The ready-money prin-
ciple, too, prevents the amateur backer from
going beyond his depth. Retting on the “ nod”
is too often the downfall of the embezzling
clerk and the absconding cashier. In another
respect, the introduction of the totalisator
its legalisation on the racecourse only must do
good to both sport and the public. Instead of
feverishly haunting city cigar-divans, backers
would be forced to visit the race-course so as to
enjoy both the open-air and the racing, while
the clubs, in addition to the dividend on belting
investments, would thus greatly gain by the
increased gate-money. This extra revenue
could he devoted to charities and to providing
larger prizes for races. Careful consideration
convinces us that the legalisation of the
totalisator, while it may not satisfy the aspira-
tions of the idealist, would do much good by
offering an alternative to something far worse.

When the Time Comen.
One of the most obnoxious peculiarities cf
Toryism is the hopeful looking forward to a
bloody war, in which the rising generation are
confidently expected to prove themselves
worthy descendants of their ignorant king-
ridden ancestors, &c., and get killed accordingly.
The knighted Wrixon, at the A.N.A. banquet
list week, was snuffling to a martial strain
when he remarked that Australian nationality
might already lay claim to great energy of
character, and would doubtless demonstrate its
courage as well when the lime came. Courage,
in the old Tory sense of the term, can only be
displayed in the wretched game of human
slaughter, and no nation is truly great until it
has left a lot of valorous carcases rotting on a
battle-field, or fed the sharks with a quantity
of maritime heroes. The possibility of a nation
being born to live and die in peace is dis-
countenanced by men of Wrixon ’s order on Tory
principle. Standing armies are to them glorious
necessities, inasmuch as their loved monarchical
system is so utterly and entirely dependent
upon an occasional calling out of the military.
Consequently, Sir Wrixon, addressing a com-
munity that seems in peril of cultivating a
wholesome hatred for soldiers and the pomps
and horrors of war, gives its boys a patronising
pat on the back, and indirectly commiserates
them on the fact that their courage has not yet
been tested with gunpowder. But the time is
sin e to come, says Wrixon in effect, and then
you can wade in. Meanwhile, watch and pray
for a chance to exhibit your pluck. Put a gun
upon your shoulder and wait for something to
turn up. Fortunately, a time is very near at
hand, when no Australian politician will have
the impudence to talk such mischievous rubbish
as Wrixon talked at the A.N.A. corroboree.
Each male baby born upon the soil is a fresh
guarantee of safety for a people who live far
beyond warlike latitudes, and can (and do)
constantly demonstrate their courage by con-
quering the opposing natural forces of their
native land, not to mention the pleasant
diversion of knocking out individual cham-
pions from across the seas. Every hour
gives Australia greater immunity from attack,
for even as wc arc increasing and multi-
plying in population-the education of Europe is
fast advancing to a stage at which wars of
aggression, undertaken by disciplined fools at a
despot’s nod, will no longer be possible.
Already, in fact, the Tory manufacturers of
our shoddy military spirit look around in vain
for any possible invasion from anywhere save
Russia, a country undermined with Nihilism,
and ready to have a grand local blow-up for
freedom at any minute. Very soon the bottom
will have dropped clean out of the gaudy fiction
which pretends to excuse the fattening of a few
swashbucklers at Australia’s expense, and the
nuisance who would fain prattle of courage
“ when the time comes ” will have to howl for
civil war, or hold his tongue.
That lying institution, the Queensland “ Patri-
otic League” has this week been bowled out
even a little more badly than usual. The
“Patriotic League” is a Tory joint-stock associa-
tion, apparently formed to disseminate false
information about imaginary shearers who
poison tanks and mythical insurgents who
desire to overthrow the Constitution, and to
remove the names of all workers from the elec-
toral rolls by hard misrepresentation and other
means. In pursuit of its legitimate calling the
League, or some of its friends, lately sent forth a
horrid tale about 300 bogus voters who
were found to bo registered as residents
of one restaurant in Queen-street, Brisbane,
and who were all understood to vote in the
democratic interest. Then the “National
Associations ” of Sydney and Melbourne took
up the painful incident, and quoted it as a proof
that the democratic party in Australia was
utterly bogus and hollow —that the genuine
“working-men” of the country were solid in
the interests of capital, and that the “ Labour-
party ” was propped up entirely by impostors
who registered themselves as sleeping 50 in a
room in low-class ham-and-beef shops. The
one thing that breaks the story up is that
there are only 51 votes registered altogether
in all Queen-street, Brisbane, and therefore
the possibility of 300 being registered
in one house looks exceedingly remote.
To get 300 votes out of 51 and yet leave some-
thing over for the rest of the street is a work
of manifest difficulty. If the capitalistic party
which circulated the story didn’t recognise this,
then it is the last possibility in the way of a
fool ; if it did recognise it, then it is a very
great and hopeless liar. Also it suffers from a
woeful incapacity to lend a plausible aspect to
its perjury ; a lie is no good unless somebody is
likely to believe it, and the story of 300 bogus
electors in one hash-house can only rank with
“ Jack and the Bean-stalk ” and similar childish
improbabilities.

When the Duke of Clarence was a very new
subject for sorrow, and the voice of the gusher
was still loud in the land, the Argus ventured
to remark that some N.S. W. shearers, who had
openly expressed a sort of antipathy to
Royalty, w’erc naturally placed in a most pain-
ful position by the death of the Duke. Only a
few days previously these miserable republicans
had refused to take part in a grand bucolic
demonstration over Lord Jersey in his vice-
regal capacity. What was the result? The
Argus set its brightest intellects to consider
how those N.S. W. republicans would be
affected in the dark hour of newspaper lamenta-
tion by their reckless policy of contempt for
the Throne. And this is how the- bevy of
biting cynics, the brilliant young bucks of the
Argus, figured out the situation : “Being thus
debarred by their principles from rejoicing with
those that did rejoice, the shearers are equally
precluded, of course, from mourning now with
those that mourn, and must be placed for the
moment in a dilemma.” Holy Canton ! Was
the art of printing discovered that men might
pul a pen into their fists to steal away their
Itrains ? The shearers who objected to Clarence
as a representative of royalty, and to Jersey
as an official substitute for Clarence’s grand-
mother, had no cause to feel ashamed of the
fact that all ilesh is grass. Besides, there’s
nothing very awkw'ard about not being able to
snivel when you don’t want to snivel, although
there’s a good deal of satisfaction for the pro-
fessing democrat (quite apart from the circum-
stance of a dead Duke) in being able to put his
hand upon his chest and thank Heaven that he’s
better off than the Argus, which is always
mourning the death of some used-up institution
and objecting to principles that arc immortal.

M‘l[. wraith hopes to prance, in his own in-

The Directors’ Liability Bill, which is now
hanging tire in the Victorian Parliament, is the
sort of Act which is badly wanted all over Aus-
tralasia. It is, as its name implies, a measure
for fastening responsibility on the “guinea-pig'’
person who now affixes his respectable name to
fishy prospectuses, scoops in his fees as a director,
leads the public to waste their money in a wild-
cat enterprise simply because his unblemished
cognomen is assumed to be a guarantee of
solvency, and then escapes in the final smash
and the criminal trial which follows by proving
that he acted through ignorance and had no
share in any actual fraud. The theory of the
Bill is that, when a director, by put-
ting his name to a prospectus, guarantees
the statements contained therein, he must
take all responsibility for these state-
ments. If they are fraudulent, then it is
his fraud ; he claims to know and to have
control over the affairs of the company, and i f
these affairs are utterly rotten, then he is no
longer to escape by asserting that he was only
a fool who attached his name on trust, and a
liar who guaranteed something chat he knew
nothing about. If the enterprise goes wrong
because everything was left to the trusted
manager, and the manager took the cash, the
guinea-pig is to be held accountable for the
losses that ensue ; he is paid to supervise the
manager, and if he doesn’t do the work that
he is paid for, then it is his own look-out. A
good, healthy law of this kind, strictly adminis-
tered, will either make directors exceedingly
scarce and hard to find, or else it will make the
somnolent guinea-pig so active that the ordinary
manager will not be able to steal anything
larger than the stamps and a little petty cash.
By the Victorian “ Purification of Rolls Act” it
is laid down that no man convicted of an offence
against the law of the country shall be allowed
to vote for Parliament. An examination into
the character of holders of electoral rights has
resulted in the identification of no more than 1
in 15,000 liable to exclusion; that this is a gross
under statement is easily proved. In 1880
about 23,000 persons were sentenced by magis-
trates, for minor offences, to terms ranging from
two years downwards, while 680 others were
awarded various terms of imprisonment by judge
and jury verdicts. Bearing in mind that the
gross population of Victoria in the same
period averaged 1,104,300 souls we see that I
in 50 received punishment for lesser offences
and lin 1500 for graver crimes. Now, the
greater portion of criminality proceeds from (lie
male sex, and as the female half section of
society is the one which is denied voting power,
the apparent returns' of 1 in 50 and 1 in 1500
should be doubled, to adequately express the
criminality of those eligible to vote; but as then*
is every reason to believe that half the liberated
criminals never take the trouble to register, or
have means enough to qualify themselves for
electoral rights, by accepting the lesser esli
mate as the true one a fair average is attained.
On the basis of grave criminality alone dis-
qualifying the voting right, 10 in 15,000 should
have been struck off’ the rolls, but on the
indictment of all convicted of minor offences,
310 in 15,000 —in other words, say 2 per
cent. of the electors are disqualifiable.
Hence, either way the costly revision courts
have blundered. Looked at from the view ot
abstract justice it would appear that the man
who has expiated his sins is quite as much
entitled to a share in the regulation of his
country’s future business as he who has escaped
justice, or he who, having no practical ex peri
ence of the laws in existence, blindly assumes
that what is must be right. And, pray, what
finality is considered desirable in this arrange-
ment ? Is a youthful error to ban a man as a
pariah for the term of his natural life ‘i And
then, again, how about those refugees from out-
side, of whose careers no record is obtainable
Shall they vote after six months residence V
“ Yes, of course,” say the present idiotic law -
makers !

The first man to suggest quartz-reefing on
Bendigo was that fine old journalist “Bob”
Haverfield. This was in ’54 —when lots of Bendi
gonians were convinced the district “ was worked
out.” The first company was, however, not
formed till ’57, the number of shares being 50 I
and the contributions five shillings per share per
week. Nearly all the primitive companies were
similarly organised ; for they meant business and
honourable business in those days ; and the wild-
cat promoter-the sort of creature who takes a
tall hand at running “National Associations"
when he has been successful in steering clear of
bigger sharps than himself —was then unknown.
Lord Compton, eldest sou of the Marquis
of Northampton, insured his life to the total tune
of .£34,500, as security for an advance of £lO,OOO
made by the insurance company, who must also
have had some security for the payment of pre-
miums. Compton was expected, in the ordinary
course of natme, to succeed to his father's estates,
repay the loan with interest, and close the
transaction. However, the heir died, whereupon
the noble Marquis, his father, sued the company
for the amount of insurance policies, less the
£lO,OOO and other moneys Chargeable upon them.
The end of the three law-suits is a final decision in
the House of Lords, by two noble Judges against
one noble Judge, in favour of the bereaved Mar-
quis. This is about the only case on record in
which a British nobleman has “ made a bit ” out
of the death of Ins prodigal son.

People overdo the smartest trick now and then, especi-
ally when they want to get loo’ard of the Custom House,

Woman is woman’s natural foe—especially when a man
comes past. Which is the reason why a clique of damsels
in a certain Southern port wrote anonymously to the new
commission on board one of the warships warning the
officers of the damsels who were snags in the social
stream, and cataloguing themselves as the only girls
whom it was socially safe to waltz with at each and every
opportunity. On the whole, it was a bold personal adver-
tisement, and the end of it was that the dashing sons
of Neptune (this is what I call lovely writing) utterly dis-
regarded the muffled shrieks of warning and waltzed with
all the pretty girls who would dance with them. Con-
sidering what a Jack-in-lhe-hox the average naval man is
when attempting the teetotum whirl, it looks as if the
anonymous black-list of damsels might have been sent by
the damsels themselves as a sort of safeguard against the
bumps and collisions of nautical partners. Girls are so
artful.
A lady just from “Home” has wept all over my
Direotoire front and implored me to warn honest young
men against the unstamped syren of England who swoops
into the Kawliny, with a yearning expression and ten
trunks, and captures a Johnnie just as easily as if he were
a fly. Because said syrens are not always, like gentle
Jane, “ as good as gold,” and a few careful inquiries (we
all remember the fond parent who lost an A.D.C. for a
son-in-law on account of the clumsy gyrations of the
private detective he set on the track of his London
reputation) us to where she “ growed ” are quite indis-
pensable before you face the altar with a Beauteous
Being who may have dropped from Heaven or come up
in a thunderstorm from the other place for all you know
about her. Now I wouldn’t have thought out one of
these suspicions if it hadn’t been for the geniiinely-dis-
I ressed woman at the peak of these sorrowful remarks.
"I have had such a moral shock,” she said. “At the
So-au’-so’s I met a lady from England whose husband
is, to my certain knowledge, alive at this moment in
London. But the hostess called her by quite another
name, ‘Sirs. Blank,’ and by-and-bye her husband—her
Australian husband, that is—came into the room, and I
knew for a positive fact that she had committed bigamy !
Her local husband looked quite an honest, open-souled
man, deeply in love with her, I could see-and there is
the reason why Australian young men shouldn’t take
every English widow on her own parole, and I want you
to tell them so.” And the moral to be deduced from her
sobs is that men should want a reference with these
British butterflies, just as women should want a refer-
ence with all foreigners. A wife with one proprietor
already is bad enough, but what security has the confiding
Australee spouse of an English lass that 14 children (not
counting the twins) will not be carefully forwarded per
P. and O. directly she has been fixed ?
There is a fashion in fortune-telling as well as in frocks,
and the latest occult boom in Sydney is a veiled lady,
“daughter of a Sultan and born in a harem,” which is
the source of her mysterious knowledge, I suppose. A
Zenana once told me that the ladies of the Sultan’s house-
hold spend most of their time telling one another’s
fortunes, though you will diffidently imagine that when a
woman got into a harem she would consider her senti-
mental future pretty well mapped out, wouldn’t you? I
had a big chat with the witch—an Oriental being with
dark brown eyes, clad in a flowing lace mantilla conceal-
ing half her face—in her mystic retreat, the other day.
She only had the old story to tell of many impatient
people indulging in a stern chase after futurity and trying
to lasso their sweet-by-and-by, in a manner of speaking-
doctors, lawyers, artists—“ More men than women,” said
the Witoh—“ but the men all come about business or
racing, while with the women it’s jealousy about their
husbands, and the girls wants to know how true their
lovers are. Let me see your hand.” I held out a dainty
palm like a crumpled rose-leaf, and the witch took hold of
it, but I don’t know what she found there, for I never saw
such shapely little paws as hers—with one taper-finger
weighted with a seal ring set in diamonds—a Royal pre-
sent, I was told. Her hands reminded me of the magnetic
little fists of Sara B.
“Signalling by jewellery” is reported to be the latest
craze—but really there is nothing new under the sun.
Every woman wears her jewellery as a signal, from the
giggling girl with “Pet” on her brooch to the fat matron
with flabby neck creasing under the diamonds—each one
means it as a pointer to somewhere. The “ Pet” fastening
is a sure signal that the damsel is engaged and that the
brooch is Gussie’s love-gift-and the diamonds are a com-
meroial way of showing that the old man is keeping a
stiff commercial upper-lip, with no present intentionof
evading the sheriff.
And what a signal does the stage-favourite make of her
jewellery as she flashes it in what Harry Shine calls the
“lime jujoe I” It signals-that your homage is a very
unwelcoihe compliment if you don’t add another glitterer
to the harvest of jewels ; and what are wedding-rings but
danger-signals that someone has been first, and engage-
ment-circlets that somebody wants to be? Also, I want
to know who now wears earrings except to draw notice
to pretty cars, and why do all diamond shoe buckle
wearers have No. 1 feet?

Considering that N.S.W. National Regatta has been
stolidly happening ever since goodness knows when, and

Talking about the Regatta, it is evident that most
people applaud a speech-maker on principle, or why
should the Admiral have been hand-clapped for his non-
committal ambiguities at the committee lunch on board
the flagship? He said “When they (of the Navy) went
to England they had much to tell of Australia, and thus
helped to make the people at Home know more about this
counti \ than would otherwise have been the case.” And
then they banged their palms at him as he sat down.
Now, his Excellency of the Queen’s Navee didn’t say what
the t.us leport about us, and, by the stony expression of
his eye, it seemed probable that he at least wouldn’t give
us a chaiacter when he’s once more at anchor in
Plymouth Sound, but still they said “Yah, yah,” all
round the festive board, and 1 felt glad to hear them.
They sounded so cock-sure, you know, that nobody could
“talk of Australia” in a fault-finding sense, and this
little fleeting revelation of the heights and depths of Aus-
tralian national conceit was distinctly edifying.
A soothing hour or so of melody liquefied by tea wr s
piovidjd by Mrs. Harry Woods, the well-known pianiste,
at Huenerbein’s music-gallery on the Block the otl e
afternoon. The palms on the platform, the walls covered
with really good pictures, and a crowd of nicely-dressed
women all led up to a more artistic wise oh scene than is
usual on such occasions-music and naked walls being
usually synonymous, in deference to the acoustics, of
course. Mrs, Woods is a timeist to the tips of her fingers,
and another virtue-she doesn’t make the composer go
her road, as so many artistes do, but follows his pegs
instead. Consequently her “bits” (Italian school prin-
cipally) made one sorry the programme only contained
three numbers.
The inky shrieks of a Sydney paper (with the pen point-
ing due Woolloomooioo-wards, of course) over the larri-
kinism of our ’arbour and “how ladies cannot go upon
the water for a ride” without being insulted by these low
characters who ought to be Flogged (with a big F) and so
on, emboldens your Sappho to rise and ask whether the
men who really insult women are the backwash of society
after all. The accepted “larrikin” is the Australian
\eisionof the scape-goat-everybody’s sins are piled on to
his narrow shoulders ! The sacred legend is that ladies
cannot go out in the evening unaccompanied, or in
certain parts’ of the city at any time, without being
accosted and insulted by the “ off-scourings of our civilisa-
tion,” but it is only a legend so far as it concerns the
off-scoured one, and no woman who doesn’t dawdle from
street to street and invite attention, has even occasion to
jump the gutter when a genuine hoodlum blocks the way.
As a matter of fact, the youth whom we have learned to
speak of as “ the larrikin ” is civil and obliging—marvel-
lously so considering his environment—and I have often
been quite touched when I asked him a question (no
woman of the world is ever afraid to do so) at his visible
inward struggle to frame a reply fit for the ear of a
“ lidy.” The person who annoys woman whenever the
chance presents itself, generally speaking, is not the
tight-trousered, high-heeled denizen of the kerb-stone
who wears braid on his coat and a tassel on his soft hat.
The real culprit is bald, sometimes with a heavy watch-
chain and a long hat, and he does the Block just about
the time the girls’ schools come out, or else he is young
and pimply and dressed in.baggy clothes and a button-
holer, and he trims a lamp-post until some girl inadvert-
. ently stops and looks intoashop window when he is heard
remarking at her elbow that it, is a lovely day, and didn’t he
meet her at Mr. Smith s? And if she is foolish enough to
admit that he did, a melodrama succeeds the Arcadian
prologue, probably, but, of course, nobody hears of it.
But because you never see a broad-cioth suit and a top-
hat laid across the knee of Justice is no reason why
broadcloths and bell-toppers should be outward and
visible signs of the white herb of virtue, is it? Nobody
points an indignant nib at the well-dressed larrikin,
simply because he passes his retribution on, as it were, to
the youth in the gutter, and when the latter is flogged or
strangled for acting like the dog of blackfellow' King
Billy, which worries the worthless leavings thrown to it
over its master s shoulder—the good, virtuous community
reads the evening papers and groans aloud over the
“ deplorable wickedness of the lower orders.”
And, carefully alluding to well-tailored larrikins, what
a rich field matrimonial advertisements and their subse-
quent sub-rosa assignments open up for the genus!
Common street-chases after possibly married sensible
women who may lead them a regular will o’ the wisp
hunt culminating in a police-station or a terrible snag < f
a hubby, can’t hold a match to the possibility of the
“ view matrimony ” advertisement. For, if the charmer
attends a rendezvous she and Lothario are birds of a
feather, and there is no uncertainty at all, though the
lady may be sometimes a little disconcerted at the per-
sonal appearance of her conquest. Nevertheless, the
sentimental ad. groweth in favour, for you can’t go to
the theatre without passing an obviously matrimonial-
ad. swain under every lamp-post. I have a vivid recol-
lection of the last one I saw-a pale-faced, middle-aged
man with dyed-purple whiskers (the dye" has never yet
been made which can’t be detected with one flicker of the
eyelash) surmounted by a tail, grey hat, standing on a
corner and ostentatiously twirling a white rose. I would
bet a horse to a hairpin she was to know him by that
virgin flower at the first rendezvous. Afterwards, I
suppose, she would recognise him at haif-a-mile by reason
of the hair-restorer,
Canst number the leaves on yonder tree ? or the hairs
on the heads of your familee? because if you can you will
probably compute just how big a mob squeezed into Sydney
Association Ground on the second day (Saturday) of the
England v. all-Australia bat-and-ball struggle. There
wasn’t a seat available on any of the three stands in the
afternoon, and late-comers like Yours Ever had to do the

and pressed ids perch upon them, which they always
accepted with much gratitude all the women say,
“ Oh, thank you so much,” now when a man gives
up his place to them—ever since the newspapers
noticed that they usen’t to. In such a raging human sea
it was quite impossible to grasp who had paid their little
half-crown, for people did not meander because there
were so many human beings waiting-to “jump” their
chairs, and the only rush that occurred was into the tea-
room at four o’clock when I saw the fetching' Marie Fraser
in a white dot muslin and a black hat with a pansy in if,
smiling seraphicaliy at a bun while her handsome cavalier
rushed wildly round for a fork. Other people surged in
pursuit of the death-dealing tart and the saddle-coloured
beverage, and in the maelstrom I glimpsed Miss Nellie
Hill, in a white jacket and a pied band round a sailor hat;
Mrs. Dick Teece in a strawberry corduroy with a white
vest, butterfly bonnet in green and strawberry; Mrs.
Sams in a delicate pale-green, mingled with cream silk,
green bengaline and gold braid, airy bonnet en suite;
Miss Sams in fawn cotele, hat in black and gold and pink >
Mrs. Francis Adams in black grenadine over shot-red silk ;
the Misses Throckmorton—the engagement is announced
of the eldest sister to Major Churchward (which is a good
marrying sort of a name)'—in white gowns ; Miss Want in
black. There were rumours, too, of a trio of titled women
and other heavy swells from the Big Smoke being on the
field, but no special halo seemed to balance over the
bonnets and belltoppers of these exalted Beings from
’Ome, so you couldn’t tree them even with a spy-glass.
Professional Sydnej’ was hugely there, and it was deeply
interesting to see an actress, who ran such a rig ever so
lang syne with one or two well-known Benedicts, strolling
confidently on the gravel in the members’ reserve, while
one at least of her former friends “ sat ” rigidly by the
side of his legal adored, who, of course, saw actually
Nobody at that moment.
In “ Forty-Nine,” at Her Majesty’s (a badly-named play
to take a virgin to, “standing with be-bunioned feet
where middle-age and old-age meet,” don’t you think ?),
Madge Seymour wore a cream-coloured ethereal evening-
dress in act last, with a diamond spray in her hair.
Maggie Moore’s clever jugglery with black velvet
streamers on her yellow gown, in the same scene, ought
to be prayerfully studied by every plump-figured charmer
who would fain avoid even the very appearance of avoir-
dupois. In the prompt-box, Kate Bishop, in cream, and
M atts-Phillips, in black, charmingly represented the man-
agement, and a weak sensation was caused in the d.c. by
a strapping pressman from Melbourne crawling under the
draperies round the vice-regal enclosure as a short dive to
somewhere.
Of the two songs “Scottish Emigrant” and “Tired
Mother” (sent to me by the composers, J. and M. Robert-
son, of Maoriland), the first-named is full of those sudden
semi quavers that make singing a Hielan’ sang, ye ken,
and dislocation of the jaw synonymous, so it ought to
boom in kilted circles. It has eight verses and the melody
never varies, so people who like songs by the hundred-
weight are confidently invited to inspect. The second
song, “Tired Mother,” is a pretty, soothing lullaby
calculated to send the most fractious infant to the land
of Nod, and ending on a note which is artistically soft and
low. Most alleged lullabies end with a yell on the top
branch loud enough to arouse the god Murphy himself. But
in neither of the songs is there originality of words or
music.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

During the last six months the Broken
Hill Proprietary silver-mine divided £576,000.
Dp to date £4,090,000 dividends have been dis-
tributed in all, in addition to £1,744,000 in shares
in off-shoots from the mine. The gross output
from the beginning to the end of last year amounts
to £7,360,000. The daily output is now a ton
of silver and 150 tons of lead.
The late Dean Donaghy, of Victoria, died
possessed of twopence. All through his long life
he usually had twopence—sometimes less—very
seldom more. When his funds got up above the
high-water mark of these two lonesome coppers he
usually gave the surplus to the poor. He left no
will—no executors—no visible fortune of any kind,
but his sorrowing friends looked into the waist-
coat pocket of the departed, and there his entire
worldly wealth was discovered. He that giveth
to the poor lendeth to the Lord, and Donaghy has
doubtless gone to collect a long bill on the golden
shore—if there is any shore.

Stories ” is where Stead prints the correspond-
ence between himself and the Secretary of the
Psychical Research Society. Conspicuous is
Stead’s postscript. Stead: “P.S.—I shall require
100,000 forms for the English edition of the
Christmas Number. I may add an equal number
for my American edition.” Bacon : “I knew one
that when he wrote a letter he would put that
which was most material in the postscript, as if it
had been a by-matter’’—-Essay on “Cunning.”
Bible : “ As it was in the beginning, ’tis now, and
ever shall be, world (of letters) without end.”
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
To the inventive genius of a lady in 1843
are we indebted for the first ice-cream freezer,
but those ladies who wish to save time, labour,
and money invest in the new German Ereezer.
Why ? There is no handle to turn, not half so
much ice used, and it keeps the cream firm in or
out doors. Two quarts of ice cream can be made
for Is. fid. to Is. !)d. Send for Sydney testimonials
to T. A. Knight & Co., St. James Chambers,
King-street, Sydney, who supply the machine.
Price, 305., with instructions so simple that a
child can understand.

At Her Majesty’s on Saturday last Maggie
Moore and William Rignold appeared in what
the play-bills called a “ thrilling comedy-drama.”
in a prologue and three acts, entitled “ Forty -
ISine. ’ There was a very good house, and the
piece presented should have a decent run, as it
belongs to that type of song-and-dance and
variety-show sensation so dearly loved by the
average Sydney play-goer. It 'is hardly calou-
Jated to draw a continuously-filled dress-circle
but managers tell you that when the other parts
of the house are crowded, dress-circles matter very
(I m, wa y' The Pi ece > s ;l compromise between
ihe Miner’s Daughter,” “ The Danites,” “My
partner,’ and “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” from which
last-named play it borrows a pious nigger with a
snuffle and a forelock of white horse-hair. The
heroine, known by the sobriquet of “Carrots”
- it s a case this time of corned beef and carrots—
has been dragged up by the keeper of a Rocky
Mountains shanty. “ Carrots ” is, moreover, an
heiress ; and a good young man, who gives himself
away at every possible opportunity, and who
wears a stove-pipe hat and a suit of Broadway
clothes in a Far West mining camp, falls in love
with her, without knowing anything about her
prospects. A bad young man, who likewise
indulges in transparent scheming, is also on the
trail, and marries the wrong woman in the hope
of annexing the millions. “Forty-nine ”is an old
miner who tunnels into the bowels of the earth
for 25 years, and who stands a fair chance of
being hanged by the Vigilantes in the abysses of
a sunless gulch for a crime he didn’t commit,
ibis picturesque and red-shirted patriarch
is the protector of “Carroty” and the father
of the good young man to whom he makes
over the millions in the tunnel on condition
that he marries the girl whose identity with
the lost heiress is established by the pious nigger
through the medium of a camp-meeting bymnlet,
upon which everybody settles down to cham-
pagne, broadcloth, low-necked dresses, broken
Trench and Delrnonico suppers for the term of
their natural lives, after the wrong heiress has
obtained a divorce and the bad young man has
been sent to Sing Sing.
• * #
Most of the business takes place in a Far
West shanty, the bar of which is fitted up like the
Australia’s ’ with alabaster pillars and hangings
of damask, and sapphire, amethyst and jasper
fixings, and various splendours far surpassing the
material joys of the New Jerusalem. William
Rignold as “Old Forty-Nine’’ has all the fat of
the show and makes the most of it, not withstand-
ing the fact that the piece is crammed with dreary
Yankee stage-gags and various literary dri veilings
of ghastly commonplace. Maggie Moore, although
somewhat too fleshy for the part of “Carrots,”
has songs, dances and catchy variety-show busi-
ness, mixed up with cheek and pathos of a
homely brew, that give her a grip on the audience
from beginning to end ; while J. W. Sweeney as a
permanently thirsty dead-beat, and G. P. Carey
as Uncle Tom, the pious nigger, are about evenly
placed, and, after the principals, deserving of
special mention. H. R. Jewett is the good young
man of the show, and has so many pious plati-
tudes, and so much romantic nonsense to dis-
burden his soul of, that it is a wonder he doesn’t
collapse before the curtain falls on the last act.
The situations in “ Forty-Nine ” are highly
dramatic, but the dialogue is feeble and the gags
are out of date.
» « '% *
The “Forty Thieves” are still at work
at Sydney Royal, but they are getting nightly
more monotonous in their ineffable gorgeousness,
and the great sovereign public yearn, like the
succulent bivalve, for something different from
the oyster-knife. The unspeakable Graggs have
added a new storey to their human edifice, and
periodically execute convolutionary eccentricities
that remind us painfully of the nightmares of our
youth. Also the Fairy Four have donned
bonnets and accepted the responsibilities of busi-
ness entirely novel, and the Captain of the
“ Forty ” has invested in anew uniform. Every-
body is flourishing like a green bay-tree, and like-
wise deep regrets are fostered that the pantomime
is coming to a speedy conclusion, and that nether
limbs will be consigned to a region unacquainted
with limelight, and beyond the fociW of the
masher’s glass
** * *
“ Uncles and Aunts,” at the Criterion, con-
tinues in a dolorously energetic fashion to heap
contempt upon the ancient perspn who wears a
wig, and to destroy youth’s fond illusions respect-
ing the sanctity of gray hairs. Cannam improves
upon a closer acquaintance and becomes more
deliciously unctuous with each time of asking. If
this sort of thing continues, R. Brough will need to
look to his laurels, and make fresh arrangements
for a new comedian. Not that R. Brough, of that
ilk, is ineffective—far from it. He holds up one
end of the show in a drily humorous fashion,
which leaves nothing to the reader of the play-bill
to complain of. One of the most artistic people in
the cast is George Lash Gordon, whose Bates,
albeit a trifle, is_as clean-cut as anyone can desire.
Miss Brenda Gibson is reasonably pleasant, and
Miss Marie Fraser tries conscientiously to be the
same. This young lady is very graceful and wears
her clothes well, but she has, unfortunately for
stage purposes, a little too much throat in her
voice, which is a handicap that even Sims Reeves
couldn’t successfully hold up against. Mr. Fabert.
advertised as a recent importation in the comedy
line, gets better with each performance, although
he will, with much endeavour, hardly ever equal
some actors we wot of, whose recent arrivalship
has not been quite so much nOtorialised.
*■ * * *
Afc the Garrick Theatre, on Saturday last,
Bridget O’Brien, Esq.,_ opened a short season of
John F. Sheridan. This piece has been so often
described, and the merits of the actors taking part
in it so frequently commented upon in the columns
of The Bulletin, that further remark is superero-
gatory. If the Polytechnic management really
desire to make the fighting kangaroo a draw, it
should match the plucky little marsupial with
somebody nearer its own size. A feather-weight
boxer would b.e an admirable substitute for the
robustious and burly pugilist who at present
mauls the gentle eyed creature about the stage.

** * »
“ Evangeline ” has been doing moderate
business at Melbourne Alexandra for the past
week or so, the celebrated sixpenny gallery of this
establishment being always crowded and generous
with its shrill note of admiration. Coming
immediately after a weak pantomime, “Evan-
geline ” seems a tolerably lively entertainment at
popular prices. Sundry incidental apparitions,
such as the whales and the heifer, don’t shock
one’s feelings as they did aforetime, when the
show was unfavourably contrasted with the
splendour of its advance puffs in the daily press.
A full-sized orchestra does full justice to Rice’s
collection of tinkling melodies, the chorus-singing
is good enough, and the general scenic effect is
assisted by exceptionally clever manipulation of
the lights” Most of the old people are still
struggling after humour in their original parts.
TheJjone Fisherman appears to possess charms for
the young; in fact, the mirth of the gallery when
that perennial dumb-mute catches a property eel
with a crust of bread is simply awful. Catherine
is back again, too, and although the huge object
in skirts is more subdued and thoughtful than of
yore she (or rather he) provides the eye with
plenty to rest upon when it is weary. John Ford,
however, is a less satisfactory foil for the stout
gentleman than Gourlay, so in this important
respect the beautiful story of “ Evangeline ” has
lost a little of its subtle humour. Otherwise the
strength of the patient is fully maintained, as
they observe in medical bulletins. Virginia Earl,
of the well-shaped knees, turns her little bit of
voice to good account, Fanny Liddiard is singing
sweetly, and the glittering amazons glitter much
the same as they did on the narrower stage of the
Opera House. Probably “ The Corsair ” will be
put up on Saturday night.

able week at the Royal, and the proprietor of the

decayed teeth through gnashing them, and that

bed, under an impression that he had suspended
himself by the Royal free-list from a hook in the

throat, and she couldn't tackle all her songs last

Shakespeare still toddles along at Mel-
bourne Bijou, and the management has taken to
announcing that “ Much Ado about Nothing ” is
not for an age, but for all time—which sounds
like an uncommonly long run for this worthy
show. Meanwhile, “ Much AdoI’has 1 ’has got as far
as its sixth week without the date of withdrawal
being referred to, so the Immortal Bard is evi-
dently backing himself to put up an Australian
record, and B. and B. are holding the stakes.

The patient spider and the small pene-
trating insect that loves to build its nest in dark
places have received .notice to quit Melbourne
Opera House. The place is getting itself cleaned
once more, and the shutters will come down on
February 13 (probably), when George Musgrove is
to open proceedings with his new Empire Com-
pany, Harry Musgrove, long-time treasurer at
the Princess’, has left the old shop, in order to
come and look after things for his brother. Things
require a lot of looking after in front of the Opera
House, and the brethren had better hire a few
sportsmen to shoot around that preserve of
welslicrs as soon as possible. ,
The Hans Phillips bene tit matinee, at the
Bijou, was a fair success. Also a farewell to
Madame Oarandini which was to happen at the
Town Hall last (Wednesday) night, promised to
pan out well. At the Exhibition Concert and
other shows there were good houses on Saturday,
and the departure of Sells’ Circus seemed to be
filling a long-felt want.

That foremost girl in yellow’s
Adored by all the fellows ;
The smaller nymph in green
Kicks wondrous sweet and clean ;
Their mashers, dressed as boys,
Are also fleeting joys ;
The darlings in the rear,
Though far, are very dear,
And all the lot are clever
And comforting as ever.
At Poverty Point.
A certain pretty little prima donna lately stamped a
dainty hooflec and demanded a rise to £3O a week. But
the great man’s dark eyes glittered ominously, and, fear-
ful of being swallowed alive, the diminutive nightingale
fled to a safe distance, then tossed her head with queenly
contempt—and sent in her resignation. But the big
person only chuckled, for had not a new and larger prima
donna just arrived in the country ?
When, a month or two ago, it was broadly insinuated
that a certain Prince made the acquaintance of (the
suicided) Miss Lydia Manton behind the scenes at London
Gaiety, the manager wrote to the press that such an
occurrence was impossible—there was no place where such
propriety was observed. We now notice that in the
report of the St. John divorce-case Miss St. John’s mother
admits that a Mr. Arthur Cohen was on the Gaiety stage
on several occasions, and it Is sworn that Cohen often
went into St. John’s dressing-room while she was
changing.
The Mrs. G. B. Lewis ben., at Melbourne Bijou, yielded
very little profit. Times are bad, also there was an
erroneous idea abroad that the Lewises were going
“ home” with a pile and didn’t require an extra hundred
or two. The unfortunate fact of the matter is that
G.B.L. has lost most of his little bit of money in the
recent panic smashes, and the laiy hopes to assist matters
by getting a London engagement.
Writes a correspondent:—“ Poor Pros, should be
warned against a back-blocks manager who goes about
the shearing-sheds with half-a-dozen people in a waggon.
He engages performers in the city at so much a night,
every night they play. The average is about one or two
nights a week. He says, “ Whenever it is impossible to
get to a hotel we camp out; ’ but when he gets the un-
fortunate crowd out in the bush the rule is changed. It
is : ‘ Whenever it is impossible to camp out we go to a
hotel.’ Once in camp, corned beef and bread is the fare,
washed down by milkless tea often made from a water-.-
hole wherein sheep and bullocks drink and bathe by
turns. The ladies must cook ami wash for (lie crowd or
be turned adrift. The last, crowd ho took out was turned
loose over 500 miles from town, coach and rail, and the
people, who had played about four weeks in five months,
had to pay their own fare of £4, out of their scanty
savings, though they were all promised a return fare to
town.”
A dramatic company visited a northern N.S.W. town
lately, but owing to the narrow-minded prejudice of the
hoteikeepers, none could be found to take them in. The
men of the show camped in the hall. They spread the
wardrobe on the floor for couches, and used the Box and
Cox gridiron for such chops and steaks as they could get
on time-payment. The ladies, however, could not be
expected to face this sort of thing, so a kind-hearted
publican took them in, on the strict understanding that
he was to be paid. Of course, the manager vowed eternal
gratitude and prompt pay, but, alas, the Boniface was left
in the soup. And now when an advance-agent heaves in
s'ght the hoots is posted at the hall-door with a shot-gun,
and even an offer of spot cash will not persuade him to
allow even a bill in the bar.
Theatrical affairs in Australia seem to he entirely on
the downward trend. It is said one house owes its land-
lords £2OOO for rent, while another—although occupied—
has not paid anything to its landlord for 12 months.

W. S. Gilbert was the originator of “ Billee ” Barlow’s
front name, at a time when they happened to be fellow-
passengers aboard ship. The damsel, who bad been Lily
Barlow hitherto, promptly adopted the great man’s joke-
let to stage circumstances, and became a novel feature on
the bill right away. An odd name is always worth some-
thing to a public performer. Young Norwood, who
recently hooked and hied away to England, was only
remarkable for being Eille. As Norwood lie attracted no
attention, bub in the capacity of Eille, he was a haunting
reminder of Irish drama and Scotch whisky.

The wax figure of Matthias Larkin can now be seen,
without extra charge, in Melbourne Waxworks. The
fallen saint comes in handy to stop a hole in the chamber
of horrors.
It was at a back-blocks concert held in the schoolroom.
The lights were dim and flickering, the crowd was dense,
the singers were mostly nervous, and the chairman was
illiterate, near-sighted, and in a profuse state of perspira-
tion. Also one of the performers got scared at a critical
moment, and fled from the scene rather than get on the
platform. Somebody had just finished a song, and the
chairman was trying to decipher the next item on the
programme. “ Mr. Smith,” he said, solemnly, “ will now
sing ‘God Save the Weasel.’” “Mr. Smith’s gone,”
ejaculated his left-hand supporter hurriedly. “In that
case Mr. Jones will play ‘ Pop goes the Queen.’ ”

significance. Just for the present Cecil finds that Time is
tearing along on stilts.

A ticket-taker, of ten years experience with the much-
Ir ivelltd Smyt ie, says that the women scramble and
light ten times as much as men at the entrance to a
theatre. This will b.‘ a trite fact to any man who was in
the rush dining the Bernhardt season and saw the women
fighting like cats to secure a front seat.
Frau Marie Wilt, the great singer, who suicided the
other day in Vienna, had an exquisite voice, and was for
many years the great draw at the Vienna Opera House.
She was very short and very stout ; her figure was shaped
like a coffin or an exceedingly thick pump ; she had no
eye-lashes and no eyebrows, and her small, grey, pig-like
eyes were buried in great mounds of flesh, while her
cheeks hung down like bags. But her vocalisation made
up for everything, and the people of Vienna adored her
till the day of her death.
Paderewski, the Polish violinist, is gushed about by a
New York critic as follows —“Paderewski's head is
‘delicately poised on a throat as white as a virgin’s;’
‘ his nimbus of hair is indescribably coloured : ‘his com-
plexion is the sulphur that lurks in the heart of the white
rose,’ and his touch ‘is original - languidly tired in
Chopin’s nocturnes, fiercely powerful in Lisztian rhapso-
dies, and velvet and golden in broad cantilena.’” Me
hope this isn’t true. It sounds like the description of the
much-bangled she-gentleman with the gold-washed head,
who has made us all feel tired at some period or other of
our lives.
Madam Marie Roze says;—“ A girl who has any regard
for her voice cannot be too careful when going out into the
open-air in her wraps, and especially in the manner in
which she protects her chest and throat. Another im-
portant thing is rest—and plenty of it. As to diet, she
should avoid sweets of all kinds. Pastry, nuts, almonds,
raisins, pickles, rich sauces, are simply poison to a singer.
As regards fluids, all are harmful, if taken in immoderate
quantities. Milk is good, hut it is best mixed with soda-
water or seltzsr. Wines of any kind are injurious, and I
cannot be too emphatic in warning all girls who aspire to
he singers to abstain from them. I have Known some of
the most promising voices to be utterly ruined by even
the smallest indulgence in wine. Fresh and ripe fruit
always make a very good diet for a singer, especially
grapes. Very often, almost daily, I might say, I eat a
pound of grapes, and find them the best possible tonic
for the vocal chords.” Evidently a voice is as much
trouble as a white elephant or a mangled corpse hidden
under the bed.
A Melbourne vocalist, married lately, recently went to
London to ooncertize, but changes her Mrs. into Miss.
Why? Are we never to have any more of the beautiful
dignity of the young matron?
Madame Marches!, Melba’s music-mistress, lives in
Paris, and is the most famous voice-maker—a grey-haired,
pinch-faced, sharp-set, snappy-voiced, thin Cardinal-Mann-
ing-looking woman. Dizzy wrote: “The critics are those
who have failed in literature or art ’’—and Marches! failed
as a singer.
