Harry Jackson used to show a scar between his eyes
from Lola Montes’ dagger, which she always carried, the
blow being delivered behind the scenes in Adelaide, but
it was meant for Folland, the comedian, who afterwards
drowned himself on this woman’s account. In Australia
she looked rather like Madame Celeste.

iamin should evidence the possession of a boundless

Our London correspondent says that had the acting
been half as good as the play itself Haddon Chambers
would have secured a brilliant success with “ The
Honourable Herbert,” performed for the first time on
22nd Dec. at the Vaudeville. As it is, he is to be con-
gratulated on having produced a really beautiful play,
which will certainly enhance his growing reputation. The
London papers are much divided in opinion concerning
the play.
The latest fashion in Melbourne “ booms ’
is the oyster-saloon row. Sometimes there’s a
lady in it. At other times Macearoni attempts to
carve an obnoxious compatriot.
Messrs Evans and Chandler, proprietors of
Adelaide Quiz, have been committed for
libelling Gerald and Duff, who are taking civil
libel-suits against the two Adelaide dailies.
Queen Victoria was, some time ago,
appointed by the Kaiser honorary Colonel of a
German Regiment. The other day a Lieut.-
Colonel arrived at Windsor who had travelled all
the way from Berlin with a servant, for the pur-
pose of personally reporting to his military
superior Queen Victoria his appointment to the
sub-command of the regiment !
Canon Liddon : “I have read something
of church history and find that the church cause
is always triumphant in islands rather than on
the mainland. Quite true. How gospel-
ground are the Norfolk Islanders and the Tas-
manians ; and look at the poor South Sea Islander
with a parson’s thumb on his carotid artery all the
time!
The N.S.W. “National” Association is
just about to promulgate a new scheme of com-
prehensive organization in which they are having
the support of certain pseudo-radicals who are
bartering their alleged principles for cash. Not
that The Bulletin blames either the Association
or the sham democrats. After all, political
organization is a mere matter of business - a point
which the workers are slow to recognise. So long
as they are content with the organization com-
posed purely of their own section of society so
long will they be beaten by money which hires
brain-power.
Chapman and Hall, London publishers,
pay <£2oo, under jury verdict, because one of
their authors clapped a real personage into a novel,
wherefore Yates moralises on this practice by
Dickens. He caricatured his own father in
“Micawber,”and his mother in Mrs. Nickleby, and
was surprised they never could see it, and he took
off Landor and Leigh Hunt offensively, in the
Boythorn and Harold Skimpole of “ Bleak
House,” while Yates himself put all his comrades
at the Post Office, by their real names, though
not portraits, in one of his novels. Thackeray,
however, was more liberal in this way than any.
We have heard Richard Birnie, of Melbourne’s
last generation, boast that Thackeray took him
off, though it was in an odious kind of way.
Then there is Disraeli, with swarms of portraits :
Bute, Manning, Croker, Palmerston, Gladstone,
&c. &c.

How Polly Paid for Her Keep.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Do I know Fully Brown ? Do I know her? Why,
damme,
You might as well ask if I know my own name?
Jl s a wonder you never heard tell of old Sammy,
Her father, my mate in the Crackenbaek claim.
He asks if I know little Poll! Why I nursed her
As often, I reckon, as old mother Brown
'Vhen they lived at the “Flats,” and old Sain
went a burster
In Ohinaman’s Gully, and dropped every crown.
My golden haired mate, ever brimful of folly
And childish conceit, and yet ready to rest
Contented beside me, ’twas I who taught Polly
To handle four horses along with the best.
Twas funny to hear the small fairy discoursing
Of horses and drivers, 111 swear that she knew
I'lvery one of the nags that I drove to the “ Cros-
sing,”
Their vices and paces and pedigrees too.
She got a strange whim in her golden-haired
noddle
That a driver’s high seat was a kind of a throne,
I’ve taken her up there, before she could toddle.
And she’d talk to the nags in.a tongue of her
own.
Then old Mother Brown got the “horrors” around
her
(I think it was pineapple-rum drove her daft),
She cleared out one night, and next morning they
found her,
A mummified mass, in a forty-foot shaft.
And Sammy? well, Sammy was wailing and
weeping,
And raving, and raising the devil’s own row ;
He was only too glad to give into our keeping
His motherless babe—we’d have kept her till
now
But Jimmy Maloney thought proper to court her,
Among all the lasses he loved but this one.
She’s no longer Polly, our golden-haired daughter,
She's Mrs. Maloney, of Packsaddle Run.
Our little girl Polly’s no end of a swell (you
Must know Jimmy shears fifty-thousand odd
sheep)—
But I’m clean off the track, I was going to tell you
The way in which Polly paid us for her keep.
It was this way : My wife’s living in Turn-
barumba.
And I'm down at Germanton yards, for a sale,
Impacting coach-horses (I wanted a number),
When they flashed down a message that made
me turn pale.
Twas from Polly, to say that the old wife had
fallen
Down stairs, and in falling had fractured a
bone—
There was no doctor nearer than Tumut to call
on,
So she and the blacksmith had set it alone.
They’d have to come down by the coach in the
morning
As one of the two buggy ponies was lame,
Would I see the old doctor, and give him fair
warning
To keep himself decently straight till they
came ?
1 was making good money those times, and a fiver
Per week was the wages my deputy got,
A good, honest worker, an out-and-out driver,
But like all the rest a most terrible sot.
So, just on this morning, which made it more
sinful,
With my women on board, the unprincipled
skunk
Hung round all the bars till he loaded a skinful
Of grog, and then starts on his journey, dead
drunk.
Drunk ! with my loved ones on board, drunk as
Chloe,
He might have got right by the end of the trip
Had he rested contented and quiet, but no, he
Must pull up at Rosewood, for one other nip.
That finished him off, quick, and there he sat,
dozing,
Like an owl on his perch, half-awake, half-
asleep,
Till a lurch of the coach came, when, suddenly
losing
His balance, he fell to earth all of a heap,
While the coach, with its four frightened horses,
went sailing
Downhill to perdition, and Carabost “break,”*
Four galloping devils, with reins loosely trailing,
And passengers falling all roads in their wake.
Two bagmen, who sat on the box, jumped together
And found a soft bed in the mud of the drain ;
The barmaid from Murphy’-efell lightas a feather —
1 think she got off with a bit of a sprain ;
While the jock, with his nerves most decidedly
shaken,
Made straight for the door, never wasting his
breath
In farewell apologies ; basely forsaken,
My wife and Poll Brown sat alone with grim
death.
While the coach thundered downward, my wife
fell a-praying,
But Poll in a fix now is dashed hard to beat;
She picked up her skirts, scrambled over the
swaying,
High roof of the coach, till she lit on the seat,
And there looked around. In her hand was a
pretty,.
Frail thing made of laces, with which a girl
strives
To save her complexion when down in the city—
A lace parasol ! yet it saved both their lives.
Oli, Polly was game, you may bet your last dollar.
She leans on the splashboard, and stretches and
strains
With her parasol, down by the off-sider’s collar,
Until she contrives to catch hold of the reins.
They lay quite secure in the crook of the handle,
She clutched them—the parasol fell underneath.
I tell you no girl ever could hold a candle
To Poll, as she hung back and clenched her
white teeth.
The bolters sped downward, with nostrils dis-
tended,
She must get a pull on them ere they should
reach
The fence on the hill, where the road had been
mended;
The blocks bit the wheels with a “ scroope” and
a screech.
The little blue veins in her arms swelled and
blackened,
The reins were like fiddle-strings stretched in
.... h fr grip,
When the “break” hove in sight, the mad gallop
had slackened,
She’d done it, my word, they were under the
whip.
They still had the pace on, but Polly was able
To steer ’twixt the fences, with never a graze,
They flashed past the “ Change ” where the groom
at the stable
Just stood with his mouth open, dumb with
amaze.
On the level she turned them, the best bit of
driving
That ever was done on this side of the range,
And trotted them back up the hill-side, arriving
With not a strap broken in front of the
“ Change.”
And the wife? —well, she prayed to the Lord till
she fainted;
I reckon He answered her prayers all the same—
He must have helped Polly, it’s curious now,
ain’t it,
To see a thin slip of a girl be so game ?
Did I summons the driver? I had no occasion—
The coroner came with a jury instead,
THE BOOT OF THE PERIOD.
Who found that he died from a serious abrasion —
Both wheels of the coach had gone over his
head.
BARCROFT H. BOAKE.
Where a mail road passes through a fence it is cus-
tomary to dispense with a gate, a lane being built on either
side of the opening instead, which goes by the name of
a “ break.”

—New Novel.
Cabled that Lord Tennyson—we must
quote the “Lord ” in this connection—has written
“a fine poem” on the death of the Duke of
Clarence. No matter :he wrote “ Crossing the
Bar.”
He is a well-known (and much loved) news-
paper-proprietor, and he dropped into the shop of
one of the suburban news-agents the other night.
Noticing some copies of the Scientific American
and other Yankee publications on the counter, he
asked the agent “why he kept those papers?”
“Keep them? Why, I get through a good
many!” - it, man,” quoth the indignant
prop., “ that injures my weekly !” Four-sevenths
of his paper’s contents are taken bodily from the
American journals—that’s where the injury comes
in.
The first religious service in Horsham
(Vic.) was hold in the police-station. The same
could be said of many other townships, but there
were two peculiarities about the Horsham service.
The preacher knew exactly six words of English—
“ Good day !” “ God bless you !” and “ Thanks !”
—and the congregation of seven did not know a
word of German, the preacher’s language. That
was peculiarity the first; and the second was that
the walls of the “ chapel ” were papered with pro-
clamations offering rewards for arrest of criminals
aggregating i!2000.
“London Day by Day” in the Lawson -
Levy’s D.T. is for the most part a dry-hash record
of the “movements ” of Hingland’s hold nobility.
It starts off with the joyful intelligence that V.R.
is expected in the metropolis shortly, and gradually
drops down to such items as “ Lord William
Nevill is much better,” and “Lady Newtown-
Butler passed a quiet night.” Perchance the
death of an M.P. may be mentioned “with
regret,” but nothing unpleasant in the way of
starvation or vulgar suffering ever troubles
“ London Day by Day.”

Among the applicants for the post of
secretary to the Victorian Agent-General, was an

Graham Berry returns with four grown-up
daughters and two children. The young ladies
are those of whom the complaint was made that
he sent them to the Prahran State school while he
was Premier.
Rev. Dr. Steel, of Sydney, was the first
editor of Meliora, a London magazine long since
defunct, but sets of which are greatly valued,
because of the remarkable quality of many of its
sociological papers.
The subscribers’ edition of John Farrell’s
poems (which originally appeared in and were
written for The Bulletin) has been long out of
print. A copy was sold the other day for three
times the published price.
CL W. Rusden finished his “History of
Australia ” sitting in the library of Wotton House,
the home of John Evelyn, the classic English
essayist and diarist. He began it 20 years before
at his own house at Brighton, Victoria.

John Macintosh, M.L C. (N.S.W.), began
his Australian career as a wharf-lumper at Port
Jackson. He now thinks a wharf owner and a
wharf lumper are not equal factors in the body
politic : The man with the visible asset is equal to
two men with no capital but their labour.
The late Sir John Hay (N.S.W.) lost
c£l(>,000 through the operations of the Mercantile
Bank, Sydney, which he helped to establish.
Many personal friends, trusting to his good judg-
ment, bought into the bank, and lost heavily with
him. Sir John brooded constantly over the mis-
fortune into which he had unwittingly led his
friends.

I*. O. Fysh, the Tasmanian Premier
started life as an errand-boy in the little drapery
store which Stevenson and Sons ran in Elizabeth
street, Hobart, in the good old days. When tin,
Stevensons gave up the retail business, P. O. Eyg],
was employed in their warehouse in Collins-street,
and he succeeded to the Tasmanian business when
the firm removed to Melbourne.
Not another man in W.A. has had equal
opportunities with Premier Forrest to become
rich. He has a big land-grant from the Govern-
ment for his exploring trip of ’76, which ho
promptly turned into cash. He and his brothers
secured the best of the land upon the Upper Mur-
chison, since sold to Victorians, and upon which
are some of the best sheep-stations in the pro-
vince.
Editor Haddon completes his 25th year in
the chair of the Argus. That which pleases long
and pleases many must possess some merit, says
Doctor Johnson. Jas. Smith, doyen of the shop,
says he has served under nine dynasties of Argus
editors, including Wilson, Higinbotham, Watts,
and Windsor, and inasmuch as eight only endured
from 1855 to 18(37, there must be some gingah in
F. W. The fat man again. We believe in the
fat man.
The writer of the London Times’ articles
on “The Colonies,” which appear each Monday
and are attracting considerable attention (among
those who are not acquainted with the “colonies ”)
for their apparent amplitude of internal know-
ledge, is, we are now told. Miss Shaw, a lady who
has never been out of England, except for a brief
visit to the Continent! On Australasian affairs
she is coached by the A gents-General and Royal
Colonial Institute.
Gerald Henry Staple is heard of again,
from Maoriland. He couldn’t see much when
he fired at G. P. Smith, and hit poor Sesnan
Walshe, and now he’s so batlike as to be dependent
on his sisters, wherefore the hat is going round,
for a worthy man, who once gave way to passion,
and was near a rope’s end, till Higinbotham
became his advocate, on a point sniffed at by
Fisher, of the Victor Emmanuel moustachios.
Sesnan Walshe, publican, was father of Walshe,
the operatic tenor.
When the great ’53 memorial (31,000
bona-fide signatures) from the miners of Bendigo,
the MTvor and Forest Creek districts, protesting
against the license-fee, was presented to Governor
La Trobe, the ex-Moravian missionary replied :
“ He was satisfied the diggers were mere grievance-
mongers, and he knew what was his duty, and
would do it at all risks. Hf 'the diggers troubled
the Government much more, lie would let them
hear how cannon could roar.” That old Victorian
identity, G. E. Thomson, who waspresent, says
that, after dismissing the deputationists, La Trobe
resumed his seat at his desk—and finished the
composition of a hymn.

A ponderous Sydney weekly has, The
Bulletin notices, been ti, ng to make a virtue
out of the fact that it has not increased its price
to agents in Queensland. Why?—simply because
it couldn’t. It has all along charged agents as
much as it could, and they won’t sell it under lid.
commission, or 25 per cent. The Bulletin lias
hitherto allowed 33 per cent, commission and has,
now that postage is imposed, brought its whole-
sale price to that of the other sixpenny news-
papers.
Watch. Your Nose!
Already the claim is made that one’s disposition
is outlined by his nose. According to the Buffalo
Commercial, large nostrils show courage. People
of fearless dispositions breathe fully and freely.
All the fiercer animals have dilated nostrils. The
drooping nostril shows histrionic talent. If the
nose also droops, it denotes a tragic power, and if
only the nostril the capacity is marked for the
interpretation of comedy. When the nose is thin
at the bridge it shows generosity, while a nose
that is thick at the bridge argues inquisitiveness.
When it is “ tip-tilted like the petal of a flower”
the person is inquisitive. A projecting nose
argues a disposition to investigate. It is ahead
of the person, as it were, and wants to scent out
things. Look at your nose in the glass and see
what sort of a person you are, anyway. If your
nose is blue you have a bad circulation, if very
pale you are suffering from poorness of blood, if
full of blackheads, which you can squeeze out,
your blood is impure, if your nose has a red color,
it may have cost you a lot of money to have got
up that beautiful brilliancy, and it may also cost
you a great deal still to keep it in repair; in
either case you are a victim of aggravated indiges-
tion caused by the liberties you take with your
stomach and liver, and must have immediate
recourse to that great regulator of all diseased
conditions, known .as Clements Tonic, which, so
far, has never failed to cure a single case which it
has been given a fair chance. Follow the example
of Cr. Alexander Tather, Esq., 198 Myers-street,
Geelong, Vic., who says : “It is a very pleasing
duty for me to convey to' you the facts of the
great benefit that has accrued to me through
taking Clements Tonic. For tiie last two years
1 have suffered very severely from indigestion,
flatulence, and I think every other horror
appertaining thereto, being subject to great
uneasiness and retching after meals, especially
after dinner, which meal I had to discontinue
altogether, the nasty sensation and retching
depressed me so much. I tried many of the
advertised cures without benefit, but on reading
of a case (cured by Clements Tonic) similar to my
own, I resolved to give the same remedy a trial,
and, after taking three bottles, the uneasy feeling
and retching were entirely cured ; and I shall not
fail to make it known as widely as possible in the
Geelong district, where I reside, and trust it may
be the means of relieving others so afflicted.”

Why not cut the N.S.W. Assembly down
from 141 to 60 men ? There are not more than 60
men in N.S.W. fit to be in Parliament. Getting
rid of 81 men at <£3oo a-year would effect a direct
saving of <£24,300.
The Australian Star tries to “ down ’’
Republican Rae, apparently in the interests of
Premier Dibbs, who also sits for the Murrum-
bidgee. The Bulletin opposes Rae’s fiscal views,
but”puts the Republic before all else.
Tom Bent is likely to get “ the knock ” at
the coming Victorian elections. Thomas can’t do
any more for Brighton, so that virtuous con-
stituency is developing broad views and a strong
regard for the interests of the country at large.
The sudden overthrow of the Parkes
Cabinet nipped in the bud a little intrigue by a
Sydney land-syndicate, to sell its offices for the
accommodation of the N.S.W. Forestry Depart-
ment. The price was to have been fixed at
£20,000.
The Governor of the Bank of England will
squirm when he reads Griffith’s latest callous
criticism of the great “god of England’s money-
bags’ : “ I don’t see in the least why we should be
in such tremendous awe of the bank. I don’t feel
in awe of the bank myself.”
The Imperial Federation League is relying
upon the seductive arts of lovely woman to win its
game. It appeals to the ladies—the titled ones !
of England to assist the “federation movement”
by extending hospitality to Australians and hold-
ing drawing-room meetings !
They have struck coal at Cremorne,
Sydney harbour, at a depth of 3018 feet. The
prospectors have the right to mine under Sydney
harbour, and under the sea. How much did they
pay for that right? How long is it to last? —and
how much does the State get out of it all ?
M‘Pliersun, who beat the Conservative
nominee in the hitherto reactionary stronghold of
Fast Adelaide, was a working printer in the local
Register office when the strike took place four
years ago. He lost his billet, was made Secretary
of the Trades and Labour Council, and got a post
in the Government Printing Office.
The National Association of N.S.W., like
the National Defence League of S.A., is really
directed by the local Employers’ Union. The
employers are pledged to sink the fiscal question
until the labor movement is crippled. For this
purpose the employers all round the Australias
are building up a big fighting fund.
W. J. Trickett (N.S.W.) is the author of
a bill to declare poor, but brainy politicians
ineligible for the position of President of the
Legislative Council. He proposes to strip the
o'ike of its sordid salary, and vest the Council
with the right to choose its President. Trickett
always drew his Ministerial screw !
G. S. Fowler, who unsuccessfully opposed
M'Pherson fur East Adelaide,* was boomed as the
“Independent” candidate, whilst his opponent
was described as a nominee of the Trades and
Labour Council. But was he not nominated
solely in the interests of the local “National
Defence League,” and did not Secretary Burgess
thereof act as his scrutineer ?
Mr. Price Williams, representing English
capitalists, has arrived in Adelaide to “mak®
arrangements for the construction of the trans-
continental railway line between Oodnadatta and
Pirie Creek on the land-grant system.” Which
simply means that what will eventually be the
main trunk line of Australia is sought to be par-
tially placed under the control of an English
syndicate.
Election dodges, like curses and chickens,
come home to roost. A certain suburban aider-
man who, in times gone by, worked the sectarian
and L.O.L. racket for all it was worth, now raises
a howl because someone unauthorised appends his
name to an advertisement whioh connects the
local Orange Lodge with his candidature for re-
election. There is no success in that game now ;
only a dead licking sure.'
The Dibbs Cabinet, like their predecessors,
decline to table the reports of the Civil Service
Commission because the evidence is not attached.
If Dibbs will address a note to the Government
Printer he will soon procure a copy of the evid-
ence, and learn if the reports are justified by that
evidence. By the way, was the evidence taken
down in longhand or shorthand ; and if in short-
hand, are the “ notes ” in existence?
Arthur Rae’s lone-hand vote in opposition
to the Royal vote of condolence, proposed in
N.S.W. Assembly, will result in his return for the
Murrumbidgee at next general election with a
bigger majority than ever. Here is a fair and
square prophecy made by The Bulletin, despite
the fact that every daily paper howls at Rae and
tells him to resign as he has degraded his con-
stituency and lost the public confidence. Imperial

We have made a collection of 53 speeches-
in which “ the Labour movement ” is held respon-
sible for either “driving away capital,” or “re-
tarding the investment of capital in the develop-
ment of the country’s internal resources.” Over
forty were delivered by well-known land-boomers
and “wild-cat” company-promoters —the men
who thrive on immoral speculation in “values”
created by the ignorance, the supineness, and the
credulity of the workers.
How Toryism must writhe under the ter-
rible blows which the London Times, from a sense
of impending danger to the nation, is reluctantly
compelled to deal its most characteristic features
—the class control of the army and navy ! The
agony of tire poet’s eagle at witnessing one of its
own feathers upon the fatal dart which has
brought it to the ground, must be bliss itself
compared to the Tory pangs. That Age-spirit -
what pranks it does make the blind fool play !
Mr. Canning, member for West Perth,
lately asked the W. A. Legislative Assembly to
abolish the members’ qualification (freehold
property to the value of <£soo, or £5O annual
value, free from incumbrance). The question was
unanimously hung up for 12 months. Mr.
Canning’s liberality is attributable to his having
been “ raised ”in the East. He was the first
manager of the Bank of N.S.W. at Perth, and
since leaving the Wales has successfully managed
a big financial concern.
British diplomacy in petticoats ! The naval
tribute of £37,000 a year New South Wales swal-
lowed under compulsion ; but an annual tribute of
£6700 to the coffers of the Imperial Institute
Parkes resolutely refused to propose, even “ by
command ”of Tummy. A ll the other provinces but
New South Wales are understood to have been
nobbled by the Imperial Eederationists. It is
now announced that the Dibbs Ministry have been

Sydney Echo opines that 26th January
will, when all the provinces are federated, become
“ the great Australian holiday,” and that it will
entirely supersede the various foundation and
separation days observed by Queensland, South
Australia, Victoria, &e. As a matter of fact, the
26th January is an inky blot on the calendar of
the mother-colony, which no number of annual
regattas can wash out. The great Australian
holiday of the future will be Federation Day—the
day when the paltry distinctions and the degrad-
ing memories associated with the petty provincial
divisions of the past will be swept into the same
grave of oblivion as the memory of Australia’s
first gaolers.
Is the Sydney Daily Telegraph a Single-
tax paper? And if it isn’t, then what is it?
About four times a week it publishes an article
in which it sools the Single-taxers on in their
wild career, and grovels before them, and tells
what noble characters they are —and yet it never
once dares to say straight out that it wants the
Single-tax. It fills innumerable columns with the
dull maunderings of the smallest of Single-tax
drivellers, and alludes in pathetic terms to the
“convincing eloquence” of every di'y-as-dust
Henry-Georgeite, but it never states explicitly
that it believes that eloquence. This sort of thing
is getting monotonous. If the D.T. hasn’t the
courage to be a Single-tax paper, and yet hasn’t
the courage not to be a Single-tax paper, then it is
a darned poor paper anyhow, and has no more
backbone than a mass of soup.
Instead of wrangling over petty points of
“ discipline-breaking’’—which, as often as not,
are mere protests against “ bounc-
ing” upon the part of some Union official —the
Typographical Societies of Australia would be
better employed in fighting the importers of the
cheap, penny, alleged newspaper rubbish with
which the Australian cities are inundated. In
the first place, this spawn of the press is made
up of stolen and pirated, or else “sweated”
matter, which no Australian literary man or
printer who strives to pay his way can compete
with ; and, in the second place, the inrushing of
this stuff thrusts beyond all chance of existence
any cheap, purely literary Australian journal,
and thus injures the local labour market. Even
Pilfer, Publish and Co., the “supplement pro-
prietor,” pay “ colonial ” wages—to boys.
In Maoriland, at the present moment,
there are 1536 laws in force, and each citizen is
supposed to know and obey all of them, fail-
ing which, there are 1536 different ways of
getting run in. The biggest record for one
year in the way of new legislation, was that of
1877, when the Parliament rushed through 142
Bills in one session, and in five other sessions over
a hundred Bills were passed successfully. Against
these, however, there were some years that were
woefully barren. In the first session of 181)1
nothing was passed at all; in the first of 1884 only
one Act got through ; in the first of 1870 the
total was four ; and in the two years, 1854-55,
only 17 new laws were enacted altogether. On
the other hand, the Maoriland Parliament has
done some healthy work at repealing old, used-up
enactments. In 1878 it abolished 363 laws in one
fell jump; in 1891 it wiped out 285; in 1882 it
extinguished 118; and in 1877 it got rid of 101.
Still the total keeps moving steadily upwards.
In 1841 Maoriland had only six laws altogether.
In 1851 it had 108. By 1861 the total had gone
up to 298. Then it took a start, and ten years
later there were 847. There were 1197 in 1877,
and then the House started to repeal them so fast
that it took nine years to get the number of
statutes up to this figure again. But, as a rule,
the country likes statutes, and the cry is still
“ give us more law.” The Ballance Government
hasn’t done much in this way yet, and unless it
wakes up and passes 250 Acts very soon, it will
find trouble looming ahead.
Dr. Hillas, of Wagga (formerly of Mel-
bourne), is dead. He had practised medicine for
40 years.

forward the scheme of Secretary Blaine and Naval
Secretary Tracy to spend 50 millions on a XJ.S.
Navy, to equal England’s.

for London Telegraph. Willie describes himself
to strangers as the best descriptive writer on the
D.T. —which is announcing a moiety.

The Bulletin notices that one of the
N.S.W. Judges has been allowing a Crown Prose-
cutor to cross-examine a prisoner (who, under the
new evidence law, tendered his testimony on his
own behalf) as to whether he was not related to a
certain notorious criminal—the bushranger Ben.
Hall. Such cross-examination is worthy, not of a
Judge or a Crown Prosecutor, but only of a police-
man, and should not be allowed even where a
prisoner has tendered evidence as to good charac-
ter, for no man is responsible for the deeds of his
relatives. We have known a most excellent
horse-thief to be the uncle of a most excellent and
upright Judge, just as we have known a most
excellent divorce-Judge to be the uncle of a young
man who was twice successfully made a co-res-
pondent.
The “labour agency 1 ' man doesn’t get
whacked near so often as he ought to do, but one
of the tribe has come to grief in Melbourne. A
red-headed man named O’Donoghue applied to
him for work, and he gravely appointed that
person to a certain situation, and charged him ten
shillings. O’Donoghue went to the job, and
found that it had been filled up. Then
he asked the labour man for his ten shil-
lings. Anybody with sense would have known
that it was safer to stir up a tiger with a
hot poker than to chaff a red-headed man with a
grievance and a name like O’Donoghue, but the
agency person had no sense. He guyed his client,
and ten minutes later the police had to haul
O’Donoghue off the top of a mashed agent, and
the excited Irish person was run in, spitting out
his enemy’s ears and hair and clothes as he went.
Then the two parties proceeded against each other,
but the court dismissed both cases on the ground
that things were about level. A little incident
like this is a pleasant oasis in the great desert of
existence, and we hope to hear that some more
“ labour bureau ” gentlemen have run up against
the O’Donoghue.
Some years ago, when Gisborne (M.L.)
was known as Turanganui, and when the wily
Pakeha had settled but sparsely amongst the
ingenuous Maoris of Poverty Bay, lived an old
whaling captain named Reid, who carried on a
roaring business in red shawls, coloured petticoats,
blankets, and fish-hooks with the natives of that
district. The equivalent value of a fish-hook was,
perhaps, a few hundred acres of land or so—Reid
made his own terms. No banks were established
at Turanganui in those days, and the captain
issued of his own, payable on demand.
One native, who had fallen out with the store-
keeper, chanced to possess about A3OO of this
paper. Knowing that the notes were Reid’s, and
hoping to inconvenience the captain by so doing, the
Maori carefully gathered the securities together,
placed them in his “whare,” and burnt the whole
lot. Fearing lest Reid should become cognisant of
his action, and take criminal proceedings against
him, the innocent savage made a clean breast of his
action to the captain, and offered any reparation
in his power. At first the captain refused to com-
promise and threatened the Maori with gaol, but
eventually he relaxed, and agreed to take 30,000
acres of the best land in the district, and let the
matter drop. Little wonder that, when Reid died,
the stamp duty in probate on his estate amounted to
,£3OOO or so. Since this took place civilization has
blotted out all the ingenuousness of the native race.

(OUK SPECIAL MESSAGES.)
London, Tuesday Night—The Cio be
commends the action of the Bank of Eng-
land with respect to. the Government of
.Queensland.
In financial circles in the city general
approval is expressed with the letter ol
Mr William Lidderdale, the Governor of
the Bank of England, to the Queensland
Government. The opinion is also ex-
pressed that the rupture between the
Queensland Government and the bank will
have the effect of injuring the credit of
the Australian colonies.
On the London Stock Exchange Queens-
land stock is flat and sellers resent the
placing of any further Treasury bills on
the London market.

Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are —land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day oi
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
James Chambers, King-street. Sydney.
Sociery
A great effort is about to be made in
Sydney to establish an Institute of Journalists on
the same plans and specifications as those already
existing in England and Victoria. Owing to the
solemn apathy of the local paper-stainers one or
two Melbourne men are taking the initiative, and
they seem sanguine of success. Possibly they
don’t know the full awfulness of the Sydney news-
paper man. Some years ago an effort was made in
the same direction, but it collapsed with a hollow
note like a bladder falling into a tomb. A banquet
was held, it being considered that the slave of
the ink-pot might be more valiant when full of
victuals; and after the food an energetic jour-
nalist got up to explain the purposes of the move-
ment. He Began by stating that it was eminently
desirable to form an association of literary
workers of all grades in order to conserve their
interests as against those of the proprie . He
was interrupted there by a horrible sound of feet,
and never even finished the word. The noise
arose through the S.M. Herald men galloping
unanimously for the door. They felt that the cold,
searching eye of “ Mister James ” was upon them,
and at the first hint of possible opposition to that
all-seeing potentate they fled into the street and
ran right back to the office.
* * *
Sydney Evening News and Sydney Daily
lelegraph are just now having a lively compe-
tition as to which can tell the biggest truth con-
cerning its circulation. The D.T. published the
‘‘fact” that it had reached “ high-water mark.”
Whereupon the News got home with the remark
that when the tide has touched high-water mark,
it begins to recede.

Last Monday’s mail carried to a London
solicitor a package containing seventy copies of
N.S.W. newspapers containing pirated English
copyright matter. And the band is going to play
shortly.
• * *
Melbourne pressmen are howling against
George Coppin, not so much for cutting down his
free list as for advertising the fact that it was only
intended for those “on duty.” Deuced awkward
for a number of dashing young journalists (and
canvassers) who used to assume a protectorate over
the drama and religiously take their lady friends
in with them to see whether the show was going
on all right. “Take me to the pantomine next
time you are writing one of your notices,” says the
sweet young lady deadhead to a football reporter
or a special correspondent on potato crops. And
when he tells the maiden that they haven’t got
him on the list she looks astounded and asks
“ Aren't you on duty? Then how do the notices get
in the paper if you are not there?” Which causes
the burst-up critic to curse Coppin more loudly
than ever. The edict was especially rough on
sundry newspaper canvassers, who love to loom
large on the first nights and obtrusively take quiet
notes of the performance in huge pocket-books.
Besides, how can they go “behind,” and con-
gratulate the new star, now that they are off duty?
* * »
On one occasion that venerable just-
deceased bridegroom, ex-Judge Josephsoii, of
N.S.W., was defendant in a District Court case
tried before Judge Dowling. After giving evidence
Josephson went up on the Bench and took a seat
beside Dowling, who, after a good look at Joseph-
son through his “specs.,” said, pointing to the/
body of the Court : “Your place at present, sir, is
down there.” Josephson immediately took the
hint and left his seat. The genial Dowling then
delivered judgment for the plaintiff, and gave
costs on the higher scale against Josephson.
* # ♦
Lady Hay (N.S.W.), who followed her
husband to the grave within a fortnight, took her
farewell of him a few hours before his death,
when the impossibility of his recovery was com-
municated to her. He was then lying uncon-
scious. She bent down and kissed him, murmured,
I cannot bear to see him die !” and, unaided,
walked into an adjoining room, where, an hour
afterwards, she was seized with paralysis. So
ended a wedded union of fifty-three years’ dura-
tion. Lady Hay was one of the wives who make
their husband’s careers.
* * #
Godly embezzlers must not conclude that,
if they follow the example of a late official of the
Sydney City Council and confess to peculations
under the influence of “ a religious awakening ”
they are going to be let off as lightly. The young
fellow in question was fortunate in having wealthy
friends to plank down the amount of his defalca-
tions, and it is unlikely that any quantity of con-
fession, if unbacked by the restitution of solid
cash, would have touched the hearts of the city
fathers to the degree of forgiving the sinner. But,
anyhow, it doesn’t lie within the function of a
municipal council to condone an offence against
the criminal law. The case should have been
allowed to go to Court, and, under the circum-
stances, there is no doubt (the facts being as
alleged) a nominal sentence only, with immediate
release “on licence,” would have been inflicted.
* * *
Sir M. H. Davies, Speaker of the Vic-
torian Assembly, at last nails with a specific denial
the persistent rumours that he has compounded
with his creditors. The Bulletin is glad to hear
this for, though it has not hesitated to ridicule the
vulgar display of his wealth often perpetrated by
this prince of the suddenly-enriched when the
shekels were coming in freely, it has always felt
that Sir M. was a cut above others of his class.
In his days of prosperity he stuck loyally to every
man who had helped him up the ladder, and did
not trap the people whose brains he bought into
false and illegal positions.

• Unhappy is he who tries to sleep on a
pillow stuffed with overdrafts.
* * *
The second prize in the A.N.A. art-union,
drawn at Melbourne Exhibition on the awful day
we celebrate, was Arthur Loureiro’s picture of a
wooden dawg with its forepaw on the chest of a
wooden boy, who lies prostrate on a smudgy waste
of white paint, probably intended to represent a
rug. This appalling work, which covers a con-
siderable area of canvas, was the art-equivalent
for a hundred golden sovereigns. It figured of
yore in the Victorian Loan Collection at the
Centennial Exhibition, so a question naturally
arises as to whether the Festival Committee
bought their prize direct from the artist, or from
somebody who gave him an order for it. In which
latter case one would furthermore wish to know
the name and A.N.A. connections of the party
who was thus able to sell off a family picture at
such a satisfactory price.

Parliamentary Officer Logan (N.S.W.) has
been officially punished for being found out in a
liaison with his hostess. He has effected an ex-
change with the clerk of petty sessions at Kiama.
His retirement from the parliamentary service has
paved the way for the further promotion of the
son of the Clerk of the Assembly. Although
nepotism was not a factor in the punishment of
Mr. Logan, yet his enforced removal from his
old position created a vacancy by which Clerk
Webb’s son must benefit.

The Adelaide clergy are making it warm
for Governor Kintore, in connection with the
Cathedral in metnoriam service for the late Duke
of Clarence. The.? maintain that the Anglican
Cathedral was used for what was undoubtedly a
purely State service. Kintore says that he would
just as soon have gone to a Wesleyan church,
which is—incorrect.
Rev. C. T. Perks, of Richmond (Melb.),
celebrates his 40th year in business as incumbent
there. They propose four varieties of testimonial
1> painted window; 2, horse and carriage; 3,
trip to Europe for self and Mrs. ; 4, purse of
sovereigns. Reminds you of Don Quixote and
Sancho when the Don promised his squire as a
reward either half the plunder of his next adven-
ture, or the colt he had at home.

Once a forged note is put into
circulation there is no telling how many people
will be defrauded by it.” Now, of course, a

* # #
The Chinese New Year was kept up in great
style in Sydney last week. The principal Mon-
golian merchants’ shops were decorated with
strange hags, on which were displayed red dragons
and silver cockroaches, while the ravishing music
of the sweet shamsheen broke in upon the ear from
every alley-way where live Chinamen were con-
gregated. Large quantities of fireworks were
exploded, opium was smoked ad lib., and “ hi-yahs”
were extremely numerous. Altogether the
Chinese “ new year” was a gorgeous razzle-dazzle
of, pom-pom playing and cymbal-banging, inter-
mixed with much opium and fan-tan, and many
banquets, with fat rats on the half-shell.

Melbourne Fire Brigade is borrowing
<£100,000 in London. Stein has the force now in
first-class form and is overcoming all jealousies, with
the aid of the new Act of Parliament. The sky-
palaces necessitate expensive appliances, but it is
evident there will yet be horrible scenes of people
cut to pieces while jumping from high windows,
by the telegraph and ’phone wires.

A RHYME OF RESTLESSNESS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Some strange, strong surgings in my heart are urging mi
to sing,
A song whose words I cannot grasp as in my brain the'
ring—
A paean of enrapturement, or a deep, wild sorrowing.
It cannot be a lay of love to trill in triplets sweet,
Since love but lingered near me, leaving me at length U
meet
Alone, the anguish of a life forever incomplete.
Nor dare 1 smite the chord that stirs the voice of Hopi
again,
The voice that erstwhile thrilled mo and spurred me to
attain
Some phantom goal, and mocked me when I had toiled in
vain.
And I to sing of Faith, who failed a friend in dire distress!
Who scoffed at protestations poured from lips 1 used to
press,
And harboured in my heart the doubts I flinch now to
confess !
Tis not a song of joy my heart interpretation craves,
But a steadfast soul in bitter strife with the weakness
that depraves,
And turns ray thoughts in envy to the dead in peaceful
graves.
When wintry gales blow wildly through the valley where
I dwell,
And the mighty peals of thunder join their discordance
fell,
I hear the same mad music in the windstorm surge and
swell.
The same deep lamentation, (he same despairing sighs,
The straining for deliv.’rance from overwhelming ties,
The passionate rebellion that ’gainst repression cries.
But the burdened clouds wind-driven in torrents find
release ;
The storm will pass away again and its angry clamours
cease, *
The morrow’s sunrise shining o’er a radiant scene of peace.
While I must struggle blindly through a life I learn to
hate
Since the sorrows come so swiftly and the promised joys
so late,
And failure aye pursues me like some avenging fate.
The chill of disappointment, oft-recurring, o’er me
sweeps,
The gloom of disillusion o’er my bright ambition creeps,
And for the shattered youthful dreams aspiring fanev
weeps.
With introspection’s bitterness, my hapless heart i s
wrung ;
I learn so late how frail have been the hopes to which I’ve
clung,
And till my way is clearer grown, my song must stay
unsung.
Hillgrove, N.S.W.
M.T.

* * *
It is said that the dispute between Lindsay,
leader of the alleged exploring expedition fitted
out by Sir Thomas Elder, and his staff, is due to
the former’s determination to travel on strict
teetotal lines. That's the whisper from Lindsay’s
friends. The other fellows will probably assert
they have no confidence in their chief’s capacity.
# * *
By latest English mail comes news that
the authoress of “ The Golden South,” that
sketchy book which affords a pleasant picture of
the best type of N.S.W. “society” of a genera-
tion ago, was lying dangerously ill, and her
recovery was deemed to be impossible. Mrs.
Thomas (who wrote as “ Lyth ”) was governess in
several “tip-top” families, and many society
dames of to-day were first put through Ollendorf
by the little lady, the expenses of whose trip to
England for medical advice are, it is no secret,
being borne by one of her erstwhile pupils, whose
great fortune is derived from beer, rents, and wool
in about equal proportions.
* * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph’s obituary of
Sir John Hay was in the main pirated from the
Australian Portrait Galley.
« * *
Good fortune has killed yet another winner
of a big consultation sweep. Much money, many
whiskies.
» * *
An Australian Registrar has recently
solemnised another marriage in high life. The
groom is a grey-haired power in politics and the
social world ; and the blushing bride was a grand-
mother several years ago. It is said to be a love
match.
# # *
More of the humours of the influenza.
In one of the Maoriland cities a rash youth and
his girl were going to be married on the 7th of
last month, but the male partner in the crime took
the disease, and the ceremony was postponed till
the 12th. He was better by the 12th, but on the
11th the best man collapsed, and out of respect
for him the wedding was put off till the 14th. In
the interval the bride’s mother broke down, which
led to an adjournment till the 20th, and then the
bride caught the distemper and the date had to
be shifted to the 23rd. On the morning of the
22nd one of the bridesmaids lay up and the other
collapsed that same evening, but by this time the
two lovers were desperate, and they decided that
the marriage should come off regardless of any-
body. But early in the morning of the wedding-
day the parson took the disease badly, and went
to bed, and the bridegroom’s father got it also.
Then the two went off to the Registrar, with no
bridesmaids or best man or anything, and that
public official having escaped the malady up to
date he put them through. They went off to Mel-
bourne for their honeymoon, and the bride had a
relapse on the steamer, and the captain had the
influenza, also two of the deck-hands, and the
stewardess, and it was an enjoyable trip all
round.

Military mourning for the Duke of
Clarence was regulated to a quarter of an inch in
the arm-band of woe.
* * *
Talk about powerful situations in fiction
or the drama ! Just think of that fratricide in
Victoria last week! The murderer, having already
fired one charge into his brother, assists a labourer
to help his victim to his bed in another room, then
locks the door after the labourer, who runs for the
police; then, evidently from the doorway, fires
the shot which kills his brother; finally suicides !
That shot from the doorway—wounded man
writhing in agony, but pleading for life, watches
the fellow, raised at the same breast, re-load the
gun —take aim. Ugh ! The finest situation-con-
structor on the stage is but a fool, after all, com-
pared with Mother Nature.
The recently-deceased Lady de Ros is
believed to have survived every officer, if not every
man, who fought at Waterloo. The last word, as
usual.
“ Samuel Olsen, a miner, died from starva-
tion in Brisbane yesterday,” says the telegraph.
“He was surrounded by filth. It has transpired
that he possessed several thousand pounds.” In
other words, it has transpired that he had filth
in the bank as well as on the floor.
Waiter (who has seen them before) :
“Champagne with your chop as usual, Miss?”
Lady : “Oh, dear no ! a glass of water, please.”
(And then it dawns upon the waiter that they
have to pay their own bill for once.)
A Trinidad (West Indian) paper publishes
a bitter complaint about the goings-on of a local
Judge. The Judge lives at a hotel and has a
horrible drunk every night; and whenever the
hunky youths of the locality want some amuse-
ment they paint his face black, fresco his hair,
or else rig him up in female attire, and start him
on a run down the street. On one occasion, being
a little drunker than usual, he appeared at his
window stark naked, and having whooped till he
raised a crowd he solemnly delivered a judgment
in Equity and then trotted off to bed. A lady
living next door tried to have him arrested because
almost every night he used to stand on the balcony
and blaspheme at large, and his curses floated
through her bedroom window and prevented her
sleeping. Also sundry plaintiffs and defendants
are raising a row, because they hired expensive
lawyers to lay their case before his Honor, and
then; instead of delivering judgment, his Honor
simply fell off the bench on to the floor and snored
heavily. Therefore they reckon that the whole
affair was money wasted, and they object to the
Judge, and want him removed, either by the
Crown or the man who keeps the ash-cart.

T. K. Pater, Adelaide Police Magistrate,
released all his drunks the other day owing to the
hot weather.
In Germany an editor has been sent to
gaol for three months for declaring that Luther
committed suicide. The Court called it “ pro-
faning the religious creed of the State.”
A Chinaman, who ought to know, reports
that in China when a “ man of high degree” dies,
his body is embalmed by being packed in tea,
which is afterwards boxed up again, and a private
mark placed on the box informs Chinamen that
this use has been made of the tea, and that it is
consequently fit only for export to benighted
foreigners. Great Scott! Which dead leper is in
our teapot to-day ?
Melbourne Evening Herald gives some
of its history. It is the oldest Melbourne paper
alive and once ran the race with the Argus as a
morning journal—written by such as Asplnall,
Michie and Dr. Evans. The three Melbourne morn-
ing papers were 3d. each, then 6d., then 3d. again,
until the Herald first lowered to a penny, but
G. C. Levey could not galvanise it, wherefore it
turned to an evening paper, as one of the Symese
Twins. Winter hoisted it, with 20 years’ work.
Strange that Melbourne has no more morning
papers than when it was half the size.
The worst enemy of all white men the
Government had to contend against in the Maori
War was got through a glass of grog. Kemball
Bent was an American gunsmith, and fought in
the Civil War. He served in the 57th (better

first having been disrated from the rank of a non-

his grog stopped for misconduct, he deserted with
a large amount of ammunition and some firearms,
and joined the Maoris. He was a determined
man, and, as events proved, an excellent General,
and he led a large force of Maoris upon his late
comrades, and displayed great coolness as a leader.
In the engagement that ensued, he shot down
Colonel Hazzard. The 57th, however, drove back
the Maoris, but failed to secure Kemball Bent.
It is rumoured that Bent is still living among the
natives, but he has not been visible for years, as he
is no doubt afraid to be seen in any town in
Maoriland. Colonel Hazzard’,s grave lies some
miles out of Wanganui, and was altogether
neglected until lately.
Dear Bulletin, —Like Bret Harte’s All
Sin, I had “the cards up my sleeve.” Because I
was “found out,” society pronounces me a
sinner, and suits the action to the word by giving
me the proverbial kick-because-he-is-down. True,
I have sinned, but before God and man I have
expiated my offence very dearly, yet those suc-
cessful and immaculate Christians who never,
never sin, keep on pelting me. A starving wretcli
asks for work, therefore starve him, for he happens
to have expiated his sin before human eyes. Such
is my case—years in gaol for a first offence, months
out of gaol, capable and willing to work, eagerly
seeking it every day, blackmailed by Christian
tongues when the coveted morsel is about to drop
into my mouth. Worse still, I have responsi-
bilities, young as well as old, all innocent victims
of my sin. They starved while I luxuriated on
hominy and graduated in crime. Now that I
have satisfied human law, we all starve together.
Such is true charity and boasted philanthropy of
to-day, with the DischargedPrisoners’ Aid Society
and the Queen’s Jubilee Fund thrown in.—Yours,
etc., No Name.
This is the plain, unvarnished story of a
S.A. railway official. He was station-master at a
large suburban station, in a district much infested
by eminent politicians. Most of them used to
have parcels sent up periodically, and the
blubbery menial used to call for them and carry
them away. He never, by any chance, had a
cent about him to pay freight, and he always
simply marched off with the package under his
arm, and said, “Mr. will pay the next time
he is passing.” Mr. never did pay; there
were about six of him and not one of them ever
seemed to remember that he owed anything. r J he
station-master was supposed to work on a strictly
cash basis, but if he demanded the money from
any of these potentates lie knew that they would
report Imn for “ insolence,” and so he let
them slide. Then the inspector came round
and raised trouble about the unpaid freights,
and the auditor wrote a letter to the
miserable official, and told him that he was to do
his duty without respect of persons, and threatened
to have him suspended. He grew desperate at
this stage of the story, and sent in a bill to each
of his distinguished debtors. Not one of them
paid, but four of them warned him that
they would report him if he repeated the uncalled-
for performance. Then, when the next batch of
parcels came along, the station-master shut down
on the flunkeys, and told each of them that the
freight must be paid on delivery. Next day the
owners of the plush-covered creatures reported
him for “gross impertinence.” The day after, he
was reduced to be master of a small country
station out in the desert, partly for not collecting
freights and chiefly for trying to collect them.
This is a hard world for any man who collides
with a big politician’s flunkey.
Down the River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hark, the sound of it drawing nearer,
Clink of hobble and brazen bell ;
Mark the passage of stalwart shearer,
Bidding Monaro soil farewell.
Where is he making for? Down the River,
Down the River with eager tread ;
Where is he making for? Down the River,
Down the River to seek a.“shed.”
Where is his dwelling on old Monaro?
Buckley’s Crossing, or Jindaboine ?
Dry Plain is it, or sweet Bolaira ?
P’raps ’tis near where the rivers join, i
Where is he making for? Down the River,
When, oh when, will he turn him back ?
Soft sighs follow him down the River,
Moist eyes gaze at his fading track.
See, behind him his pack-horse, ambling,
Bears the weight of his master’s kit,
Oft and oft from the pathway rambling,
Crops unhampered by cruel bit.
Where is he making for? Equine rover,
Sturdy nag from the Eucumbene,
Tempted down by the thought of clover,
Springing luscious in Riverine.
Dreams of life and its future chances, .
Snatch of song to beguile the way ;
Through green crannies the sunlight glances,
Silver-gilding the bright “ Jack Shay.”
“So long, mate, I can stay no longer,
So long, mate, I’ve no time to stop ;
Pens are waiting me at : Mahonga,’
‘ Bluegong,’Grubben, and Pullitop.
“ What! you say that the River’s risen ?
What! that the melted snow has come ?
What! that it locks and bars our prison ?
Many’s the mountain stream I ve swum.
I must onward and cross the River,
So long, mate, for I cannot stay ;
I must onward and cross the River,
Over the River there lies my way.”
One man short when the roll they're calling,
One man short at old “ Bobby Rand's ” ;
Heads are drooping and tears are faking
Up on Monaro’s mountain lands.
Where is he making for? Down the River,
Down the river of slimy bed ;
Where is he making for? Down the River,
Down the River that bears him, dead.
Barcroft H. Boake.
: The Murrumbidgee.
f Snowy and Eucumbene Rivers.
There are 80,000 barmaids in England
whose hours average 14 daily, for a wage of 10s.
per week.
A I‘2o-head battery is being erected at
Langlaate mine, South Africa. This mine, like
the Robinson, is expected to return 10,000cz. a
month from 14,000 tons of quartz. The cost of
crushing will not exceed 3s. (id. per ton.
The Kiama (N.S. W.) bakers have, under
the new tariff, raised bread from 3d. to 4d. per
21b. loaf. Now, the flour-duty is only A 1 per
20001 b. This increase of a penny per loaf thus
gives the grasping bakers =£s 16s. Bd. more profit
on each ton of flour. Competition should remedy
this.
The new illustrated furniture catalogue
issued by Messrs. John Lawler and Sons, of 608
George-street, Sydney, is, without doubt, one of
the finest and most complete volumes cf its kind
ever published in Australia. It is a publication
of nearly 90 pages and contains close on 400
illustrations showing all varieties of goods dealt in
by the firm. Even this, however, is only a
supplement to a larger and more imposing
book with 750 illustrations, which supplies quo-
tations for every article beneath the sun that can,
by any possibility, be classed under the head of
furniture. Mr. Lawler has just returned from a
tour round the world, in the course of which he
has made immense purchases of all kinds of
novelties, and in addition to the large importations
made by the firm it possesses extensive factories—
recently enlarged and furnished with much
additional plant—from which all descriptions of
goods are turned out. Lawler and Sons’ ware-
house is one of these huge structures which have
arisen so plentifully of recent years—a towering
edifice which is a Sabbath-day’s journey from
basement to roof and a long and exhausting tramp
from one end to the.other. The stock within is the
largest and probably also the cheapest of its kind
in New South Wales. The vast building is
choke-full of novelties in carpets, furniture,
bedding, floorcloths, linoleums, ornaments, iron-
mongf ry, crockery-ware, and other things without
number. The one fixed object of the proprietors
is evidently to break all records in their own
department of business, and they have attained
so very near to their purpose that the rest doesn’t
matter.

The first volume of Oliver’s N.S.W.
Statutes begins with *' aborigines ” and ends with
‘justices of the peace.”
We see in one of the papers a big black
heading “ Retiring Aldermen.” This is as absurd
as Police Intelligence.”
Saxukday Review (about Brazilian Dorn
Pedro) : “ A king who won’t shoot at shooting-
tune ignores the most important part of his office,
and deserves to lose it.”
Home News says Parkes keeps the six-
pence he first earned in Sydney, for holding a
horse. So very like him. Beautiful legend, hand
it down to posterity. Charles Surface, the ex-
travagant, wouldn’t part with his uncle’s portrait.
Toby Barton was reminded by Parkes the
other night, in N.S.W. Assembly, that he was
educated at Sydney University partly “at my
expense. ” What Parkes ought, to have said was—
Partly at the expense of my numerous creditors.”
“ Prior to the advent of the Maoriland
blackleg shearers on the Darling, (N.S.W.) fleas
and Scotch greys were unknown on that river.
Now the huts on several stations swarm with
these vermin. There can be no doubt as to their
being of Maori origin, for as they move about over
the floors, tables and bunks, they can be seen
rubbing noses together,” remarks a shearer cor-
respondent.
There was only one woman on the
Murchison (W. A.) goldfield and she suicided the
other day, and now there is no woman at all. We
wonder what her name was. You bet she was
either, “The Speckled Hen,” or “The Murrum-
bidgee Barge,” or “The Bull Pup,” or “The
Bong-handled Shovel,” or “Square Sarah,” or
“Gentle Annie,” or “Mother Coppernose,” or
“ Portwine Lizzie or something like that. The
only woman on a goldfield is nearly always one of
these things.
Passage from Miss Braddon’s latest novel,
One Life One Love,” page 243, in which a
highly-educated girl is writing in her diary about
her brother :—
i5y this time lie has fallen in love with some Australian
gnl, a powerfully-built creature who can ride buck-
jumpers and camp out in the bush without fear of the
consequences. I fear I have very narrow and insular
ideas about Australia which I can only picture to myself
as one vast jungle tempered with Convict Settlements.
And yet they know all about us in Bull Land.
NERVOUS DEBILITY.
There are at the present time many remedies in
the market for this terrible complaint, but they
are not always what they are claimed to be by
their proprietors. It is, however, pleasant to
find that some of them are really genuine. The
very best of them is Herr Rassmussen’s (the
celebrated Danish herbalist) Denmark Vitality
Fills, which have cured a world of nervous
suffering, and which are all what they are claimed
to be—a genuine cure for nervous debility, des-
pondency, errors and vital weakness. They are
sold at as. Gd. and 22s. Gd. per box, posted 6s. and
2«D. Gd., and forwarded to any part of the world.
They are highly recommended to nervous
sufferers, as well as Herr Rassmussen’s Den-
mark Lock Cure Pills, which are a genuine cure
for all diseases of the blood, sores and discharges.
A fine constitution may be ruined by simple
neglect. Many bodily ills result from habitual
constipation. There is no medicine equal to
Ayer’s Pills for restoring the system to natural
and healthy action.

“Rolf Boldrewood ” is likely to pay a
visit to the Tasmanian silver fields. One of his
sons is a leading broker at Zeehan.
The Sydney News Syndicate (of 29 Eliza-
beth-street, Sydney), is a new departure in Aus-
tralian journalism. It is an organisation of
some of the cleverest pressmen of the city formed
for the purpose of supplying weekly or daily
letters to the country press, the said letters con-
taining all the noteworthy information of the day,
written up in the most interesting fashion. For
the benefit of the impecunious editor who
wrestles with the gigantic turnip in the
bush, the syndicate will supply from one to two
columns of good reading matter for 5s., and will
thus furnish the scribe of the wilderness with all
the benefits and the dignity of an expensive
Sydney representative at less than half ordinary
Chinese rates. Mr. Frank Barnes is the local
manager of the syndicate, and any country press-
man who wants to lend an air of distinction to his
columns would do well to communicate with him
forth wi th. —A DVT.

QUEENSLAND.
NOTICE.
Sale by Auction of Crown Lands in La roe
Portions.
IT is notified for public information that, under
the provisions of “ The Special Sales of Land
Act of 1891,” Country Lands in the Colony of
Queensland, distant not less than twenty miles
from a navigable river, or from a railway, or from
the surveyed line of a railway for the construction
of which money has been raised by loan, may be
offered for Sale by Auction, in portions not exceed-
ing 5120 acres. The upset price will in no case be
less than 10s. per acre.
The time for payment may be extended to a
period not exofeeding three years, instalments
payable later than six months from the date of
sale bearing interest at 5 per cent, per annum.
Persons desirous of having Crown lands offered
at auction are invited to apply to the Under
Secretary for Public Lands, Brisbane, or any
Crown Lands Commissioner.
A. S. COWLEY,
Secretary for Public Lands.
Department of Public Lands,
Brisbane, 28th October, 1891.

SPORTING NOTIONS-
How is the backer of turf “form” to keep a
roof over his miserable head? At Caulfield (Melb.) the
Ballarat horse, Annesley, won the Anniversary Handicap
of 1 mile 3 furlongs like a streak of lightning. After
l,eing last of the field for a mile the horse came forward
at the critical moment and cantered home in front of
Propounder and Tantallon. Three days later the three
horses ran over exactly the same distance at Wilhams-
fo\vn, and Annesley, who was meeting the others on only
six pounds worse terms than before, managed to finish
two or three lengths behind Propounder. The charitable
presumption is that the handsome Neckersgat colt is a
‘‘rogue,” or had suddenly gone to pieces, just as he
“ went off ” prior to last year’s V.R.O. Derby.
# * *
Tattersall’s Cup sweeps were well advertised in
the Walhalla (Vic.) district in ’9O. Two men went
“halves” in a 50,000 at 10 consultation, and drew
Carbine. A dispute arose over the matter, and the busi-
ness was settled in court, much to the joy of the lawyer.
Among the witnesses was a Scotchman, Jamie M‘N— —,
well-known in the district. Jamie had had a toothful or
two, but was very canny in the witness-box. “ Are you
drunk, sir?” bawled the infuriated cross-examining barris-
ter. “Na,na,” said Jamie, “I’m no drunk,’ addingthathe
knew of only one man who ever teas drunk in his life.
“Who was that?” queried the lawyer. “ Jonah, and that
was when the whale drunk him.”
# * *
At the Victorian Amateur Turf Club meeting
last week a balancer turned up. This is a cash-fielder
who passes an Insolvent Act for his own ben., and tries to
compound with a dividend, but the club wouldn’t have it,
and made him give a bill of sale over his furniture, to
pay all hands. "Then is Doomsday near. We are going
up a mountain to wait for Gabriel, with the Peculiar
People - #
After Sydney Tattersall’s Cup, the local D.T.
(the demon comp., of course, did it) told its readers that
at a certain part of the race “Paris/affeuedhis backers.’
The Bulletin may be excused if it remarks that Pans aid
that same again once more on Tuesday. Flattened ’em
clean out, in fact, in the Anniversary Handicap ! Many
a true word, &c. Of course Bob Mostyn wrote “flattered.’
The corap. spoilt it, but he got there just the same.
* * *
A prize story of the prize-ring. Two prominent
Sydney pugs, did Queensland together. They met per
arrangement in a Far Northern town and worked up a
terrible contest in the local paper. The foes agreed, sub-
rosa, that A., the better man, should go down. Accord-
ingly B. laid every cent he had on himself. In the battle
A. slaughtered B. and cleared with the big win. _ A. now
boasts of the “double cross” he worked on his whilom
partner, who at present is penniless in a strange town.
« * #
Some of Lord Sheffield’s “ amateur ” cricketers
were quite carried away by their feelings at Melbourne
tug-of-war tournament. While Norway was pulling
England all over the place, they sat silent and terribly
solemn, but w-hen Scotland, on the brink of defeat, lugged
Sweden all the way back to pistol-shot, their joy was
electric and conspicuous. When Ireland eloped with
America, they grew hysterical, and lurched about sym-
pathetically with every movement of the teams.
# * *
Lately two Sydney professional athletes visited
a marine suburb in a state of “ tonic.” When in the baths
one essayed the foolhardy teat of swimming out beyond
the rollers which broke into surf on the rocks. He
plunged boldly enough into the grave awaiting him and
next instant became powerless in the breakers, and was
battered to insensibility- By a mere chance three or four
bathers were enabled to rescue him. He was badly
lacerated and bruised and remained unconscious for som e
time. Moral, never wade beyond your ankles in whisky.
• * *
The Sydney Association Ground Committee-
rooms have been up till very lately a perfect hotbed of
genteel loaferdom. However, since the trustees have
taken the pavilion bar into their hands and work it as a
club they have dropped the gratuitous “nip” in the
committee-rooms, free grog has been curtailed, and con-
sequently the crowd has fallen off woefully. The irrepres-
sibles have now transferred their operations to the
pavilion, and at lunch-time the fun commences, when the
great array of well-dressed cadgers can be seen button-
holing the officials for free-lunch tickets.
# * *
It is the ambition of most N.S.W. cricketers to
“break the clock,” and the Australian who went nearest
to the feat was the old-timer, Monty Faithful!, who on
three occasions went perilously close to putting the ball
through the face of the old Albert Ground (Sydney) clock.
We give this for the edification of people who may not
have quite understood the wag who facetiously advised
Alec. Bannerman in the recent big match “ not to break
the clock.”
# * *
The Y.R.O.’s latest move is an adroit one.
While swooping down on unregistered meetings, it opens
its arms to pony-owners willing to return to the fold.
Two remunerative pony-races will in future be given at
each meeting by the registered clubs. This invitation
will no doubt be gladly accepted by owners of all ponies
worth seeing. There will now be little attraction on the
small suburban courses ; trotting is now loathsome in the
eves of the Southern world.
# # *
The bona fide cab-horse Shadow won the cab-
horse trot, at Lillie Bridge (Sydney), on Saturday night,
in 3min. 42sec. Not bad value for one “ Bishop Smith,”
the use of Shadow, trap, and driver, for fifteen minutes.
* * *
A sarcastic and irreverent person remarked, as
Bob-cab Smith came on to Sydney Association Ground on
Monday that Saumarez had probably come “ to field for
Moses.”
* * *
Moses is the Carbine of the cricket-field. He
has a recurring leg.
* * *
Australian Eleven Trott works in Melbourne
0.P.0. When he was dismissed for one, in the second
innings, Postmaster-General Duffy told his deputy, with
a twinkle in his eye, that they couldn’t give a man con-
tinual leave who disgraced his province with a single, and
that Trott had better be sent up-country.
# * *
The great Grace scored one run only in two
innings against the 22 Victorian juniors. In the first
innings he was bowled for a duck.
# # *
Considering how the Englishmen crowded
round Bannerman to make the best of his stonewalling
tactics, it is wonderful how he had sufficient patience to
block in place of trying to knock their teeth out with a
loose one now and then. .
« # *
Turner is generally the mortal cricket foe of
Johunv Briggs. But on Saturday, in the crack’s first
over, the sturdy little professional smote him for eight
runs.
# * *
The reason why a brilliant batsman sometimes
disappoints his admirers is because he has had no sleep
for two nights previous to the match. Yet Sammy Jones’
record has often been—3 a.m., long beers and German
sausages; 3 p.m., 100 runs-
# * *
A. Trott, bowling for the South Melbourne
Club, the other day, killed a swallow. Fieldsmen have
been known to oatch a swallow, but this is the first ball-
bird shot on record, and consider the millions to one
chance against it.
• • •
Lohmann’s 20 wickets for 124 runs against the
Victorian juniors was a fine exhibition of sustained
bowling form, better even than Attewell’s 10 wickets
for 48.
# # #
Grace and Co. were the means of upsetting the
old-established notion that “junior” cricket is a great
sporting institution in Melbourne. To begin with, there
was but a moderate attendance .at the 12 v. 20 match on
East Melbourne ground, proving that junior contests are,
In the ordinary way, supported by boy partisans of the
various clubs, rather th»" by admirers of the game in its
highest stale of development. Secondly, it was evident
that the two or three thousand admirers of cricket as she
is played on matting had come there to worry the
Britishers. They jeered old man Grace, protested against
the possibility of any junior (youthful or fully matured)
being out 1.b.w., and tried their level best to disconcert the
visitors in the field. The larrikins have got hold of junior
cricket, seemingly, and the genuine enthusiasm that it
was said to call forth is nothing better than a parochial
spirit of rowdyism. W. G. ground his teeth when he
found that by insisting upon knocking off play at two
o'clock on the third day he had robbed the boys of a well-
deserved licking. By the way, it would be interesting to
know why, in this particular instance, a splendid turf
wicket was, as a matter of course, covered with matting,
purely in the interest of the juniors. Under, or rather
over, such foreign conditions the Englishmen can t be
beaten on their merits, any more than Jack Roberts could
be satisfactorily taken down at billiards by an Australian
who stipulated that the table should have a covering ot
linoleum.
• * *
The North Adelaide cricketers are feeling a
pain just now. With the Giffens and Jack Lyons away
from the Norwoods, they reckoned they would totally
annihilate the latter. The Norwoods made 120, and the
Norths went in gaily to wipe the score off in an hour.
They met a second eleven bowler, however, and were all
out for 88! Adelaide has now decided that only an
earthquake will shift George Giffen’s team from top
place.
r # * #
Grace’s average for all matches in Australia,
exclusive of this last international, has dropped from 40
to about 30.
# * *
All cricketers who have met W. G. Grace hold
the same opinion. Off the field an endurable personage.
On the field he plays the game like a professional card-
expert; the veteran is master of every form of “ bluff,
and appeals on the slightest pretext.
# * *
Walter Giffen in place of Gregory ! And why i
Because brother George won’t play unless brother Walter
does. When will Walter Giffen ever play an innings like
Gregory’s 44 against Victoria? And how often does a man
twice Gregory’s size lead off with six fourers as the little
bantam did in that innings ?
# # *
The argument that skill and “ dogged pluck ”
go for as much as avoirdupois weight in a tug-of-war
holds good within certain limits. In the Melbourne con-
test the heaviest team, with a pull of from six stone to
twenty-three stone over all other lots of tuggers, won
easily, whilst the three lightest “ nations” were the most
hopelessly beaten. Judged by the weight scale, Germany
made the best fight, whilst the most noticeable feature of
the whole business was the rapid demoralisation of the
’Mur’kans under adversity.
* * *
Things grow deader and deader in the English
sculling world. The latest contest for the Sportsman s
Challenge Cup was between East and Perkins, a couple of
dried-up chestnuts at the game. Perkins was still weak
from an attack of inflammation of the kidneys, and East
won by two minutes.
. # •
WHITE WINGS.
Dear Bulletin, —I was delighted to read in a
daily paner that N.S.W. National Regatta on Anniversary
Day turned out “a complete success, notwithstanding
the absence of those sailing craft which were working for
the money prizes given by the coloured sail brigade.” I
consider the attempt to vulgarize a noble sport, and turn
a healthy pastime, such as yacht-sailing, into an oppor-
tunity for gambling, is one of the most disgusting
symptoms of the money-grubbing spirit of the present
day. Nothing is more beautiful than the broad white
sails, symbolic of the winged sea-bird, sweeping over the
bosom of the waters, and the be-daubing of their snowy
surface with green moons and crimson crosses, and other
motley foolery, though it may assist the non-yachting
regatta nietallician to make up his book is neither pleasing
to the aesthetic sense, nor gratifying to the social moralist.
Neither does it assist the genuine yachtsman in making
out the craft, for there is not a boat on the harbour whose
rig, mode of construction, and manner of sailing, are not
well-known to those who are interested in aquatic matters.
The spirit of gambling has done no good to the manly
game which cricket used to be, and now it is sought to
turn yachting into a game of chance. It may be urged
that it is better for people to gather in mobs on the cliff-
heights, and on hoard harbour steamers, in the healthy
sea-breeze, than in the heated atmosphere of a gambling-
hell, or amid the debasing surroundings of the modern
racecourse ; but one cannot help regretting the disfigure-
ment of handsome craft, and the docketing of boats like
horses paraded in a saddling-paddock. One can tolerate
the red-handed sails of the Levant, but the marine clowns
that indulge in their gyratory buffooneries on the waters
of the harbour are the reverse of beautiful, and can only
subserve the gambling instincts of a people steeped to the
lips in this particular vice. Very soon there will not be a
single pastime, or a common act of everyday life, which
will escape this general curse.—Yours, Plain Sailino.

“ Black ham won the toss.”—Superfluous to
report this henceforth.
# * *
Abel’s 132 (not out) was the best performance
seen on the Sydney cricket-ground for a long long time
though perhaps Lyons’ 134 was a more dashing exhibition.
Abel went in first with W.G., and gave a chance. Then
he made 132 without giving a chance. What cricketer
does not remember Moses’ famous innings against Vic-
toria? The third ball Moses received he spooned into
point’s hands which dropped it. Then Mr. Moses, before
his light went out, complacently compiled 288 runs.
Sheffield gave Abel £5O for his performance, and promised
Lyons a handsome trophy if he broke Abel’s record.
Lyons took that trophy by just two runs.
* * *
W. R. Wilson has seven horses engaged in the
Newmarket Handicap and five in the Australian Cup.
On form, he seems to hold a mortgage on the double.
Pygmalion and Strathmore, eh?
# * •
The Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club is to be
resurrected on new lines. Lower subscription and more
contests.
* * *
Mr. “ Bill ” Long must have rejoiced when
Birinji, the son of his old Champion Grand Flaneur,
romped home winner of the Nursery Handicap.

won’t lay about pony-races.
