# * *
A Sydney boxer remarks that the man who hit*
Jackson on the head might as well hit a stone post with a
dish-cloth. The darkey has never forgotten the body
blow from Farnan which stretched him out. Powell,
Jackson’s second in that fight, says it was such a red-hot
’un that he looked for it to come through on the other
side.”
# * *
In the second International cricket match,
Lyons wagered £5O to £lOOO that ho would put up 200
runs in both innings. He scored 49 and 134 —183, thus
missing the money by 17 runs. However, he won a wager
of £lOO to £lO (laid when his first innings score stood at
42) that he wouldn’t top Abel’s first innings score.
APPRENTICE JOCKEYS.
There have of late been strong complaints about
bad riding in many important races, and curses both loud
and deep have been fired at the riders of many a well-
backed animal. After a big event recently, a first-class
judge said that had So-and-so taken oft the bridle of a
certain crack horse, and carried it in his hand, the horse
would have won the race by half-a-distance. Keeping
hold of the strings enabled the jockey to steer his mount
into fix after fix, from which it was impossible to free
himself. He thus lost ground repeatedly in numbskull
efforts to get through on the rails, and then set to and
cut the noble animal up most brutally. Of another so-
called crack, it is said that rather than cut inside, it all
the field were out at the palings, he’d try to get round
them, instead of cutting in on the rails. It is lucky that
all grown-up jockeys are not like these two. If they
were, the ring would be able to lay better odds against
favourites.
One great factor in keeping our best and most
promising riders back in the exercise of their profession
is the gross and often dishonest use made of apprentice
lads. There are scores of these children bound for four
or five years, practically without pay, to the trainers.
Their masters, to all intents and purposes, own them, body
and soul, and while they spend the best years of their life
in a penniless condition, they get nearly all the riding,
while really good, unattached horsemen are forced to
stand down and starve, or next door to it.
# # *
In every way this suits the trainer. He calmly
charges the losing-mount fee to the long-suffering owner,
and puts it in his own pocket. When, by chance, one ot
these lads wins a race, the trainer charges the owner with
the winning-fee and gravely adds: “I gave Jim (.tnc
boy) “ a score for himself on your account, and the
patient owner turns that out also with a weary sigh. Or
course the £5 and the £2O sink restfully into the capa-
cious pocket of Mr. Morning Gallop, and the boy gets
nothing once more. By the time he is free of his chains
he has got too heavy to ride the good thing in a rich
handicap, and has to stand around and see fresh young-
sters helping to enrich Mr. Gallop, while he may be
actually in want. The lad who has put hundreds ot
pounds into his master’s pocket may go to the devil tor
all that master cares, when he is no longer a milch cow,
but wants paying for his services. He may even be just a
handy weight and possess all the qualifications requisite
to ride a good thing. Does he get the mount. .Not
likely! He sees little, 12-year-old Bob. Rubbeidown put up,
loaded down into the horse’s lungs with three or four stone
of inanimate lead. If the horse wins, despite of these greed-
prompted methods of stopping him, the trainer oleais
anything from a pony to a century in fee and ‘ present to
the boy,” and it is all dead “hunoe.” Thus real jockey s
are almost robbed of their occupation, while children aie
cheated along with owners, and baby lives are daily risked
to gratify the insatiate greed of professional trainers.
» # *
This evil has another phase, and one which also
deserves the grave attention of the jockey-clubs. Any-
one gifted with ordinary powers of observation must see
that nearly every accident which occurs at Australian
race-meetings occurs in handicap events, and is almost
always caused by some scared, nervous mite, utterly
unable to control his mount, and generally put up as a
means of extracting money from the owner of the horse.
These atoms can only hope at best to stick on, and it
there is a crush or a bad corner, they have neither the
knowledge nor the ability to negotiate it, and down goes
something with usually something else atop of it. The
best proof that it is the children who cause the accidents
is the absence of disaster in welter, weight-for-age, Dei ,
Leger, and two-year-old races, not handicaps. An acci-
dent in one of these events is almost an unheard of occur-
rence, simply because qualified men have the mounts. A
rule limiting the amount of deadweight a horse shall be
allowed to carry would have a good effect in, at least,
modifying this crying evil, an evil that presses on both
“ bound boys ” and capable riders almost equally.

Latitude.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Let not the captious critic and quasi-scientific
.trig begin again his homily, wherein the “1 ” is
>redominant. 1 say this because, in my little
'tory, I may range considerably, my instruments
>eing slightly out of adjustment, perhaps from
nfluenza, and again, maybe, it might be, possibly,
rom a deflexion of moral rectitude and conse-
piently veracity.
Itver since I served Her Most Gracious, at two-
ind-six a day (paying .£125 for board and educa-
tion), at the Royal Military Academy, Woolwich,
there has been a never-ending tremor in my soul
that s good, taken from Popular Quotations,
: irice one shilling), which has informed me that
(print it carefully) ZD + Dec = Lat. This is
rather prosaic and mathematical for a love story,
but Colonel or General, and especially Dicky Dray-
son, is responsible for it. Now, reader dear, don't
fling my yarn aside in disgust, because I’ll explain.
1 must tell you about Drayson. I have tried
Europe and the other continents, and conse-
quently have met many people of sorts Of the
lot I tix upon Col. (possibly now General) Dray-
son as the cleverest man 1 ever met, although I
place Dr. Bonzano, of New Orleans, as the most
usefully scientific.
Now for my friend Drayson, Professor of Sur-
veying at the R.M.A., author of Military Sur-
veying, also of “ Pre-Adamite Man,” and latterly
of the book on whist; inventor of a cooling pro-
cess, which was applied to the Chiltern cable-ship
which came to Australia; patentee of innumer-
able inventions ; the best billiard-player (barring
Hewlett) in the British Army. What more do
you want? And this man quietlv stood up in the
octagon at the shop (Royal Mil. "Acad.) to tell us
—“Now, gentlemen, if you happen to be lost at
sea and are possessed of a sextant, a reliable
watch, and a nautical almanac, you can find your
no 3 l tion by remembering the formula as quoted
above.
.j>veet reader: I have been lost at sea and on
land. Unfortunately, I never possessed the
reliable chronometer ; I despise sextants and
acknowledge nothing under a transit-instrument;
the only value of Dietrischen and Hanney’s
Nautical Almanac lies in the army list. And
still I honour and respect Dicky Drayson.
Here let me go out into the night to breathe a
quiet curse on the subject of my story, whom I
lately saw.
Chap. Alpha and Omega. That woman.
On board the yacht Bedouin, the house-boat of
my cousin, John William Lynns. A merry party,
slightly damped by a blue-stocking of a girl
called Jardine. And the scene is the Kyles'of
Bute. One cannot choose a better place for the
commencement of love. Later on I’ll tell you
where to fix on the end.
This woman had some money of her own and I
had none or next to none. What’s the use of
a hundred a-year and your pay of five-and-seven-
pence a-day, when you have the ambition and
temporary possibilities of a thousand ?
I coveted Jane Jardine’s modest lac: and that
is the Hindi for ten thousand pounds.
But I loved Janey J. Confound her, she pre-
tended to love me.
I said she was a blue-stocking. She wanted to
learn navigation, and for two weeks I tried hard
to teach her Drayson’s formula that the zenith
distance, plus or minus the declination, will give
the latitude.
On board the Bedouin, they called Janey my
zenith because the idiots heard the word so often.
Then an Australian stepped on board at Helens-
burgh. He was a merchant. All Australiatis are
merchants or squatters or C.M.G.’s.
My word (expression learned in Australia), he
could yarn and spin a cuffer about his stores and
business at Saint Tiberius, in Yicadelaide.
About this time Janey Jardine discovered that
her intellect was not capable (mark that word) of
Nome’s (Epitome or Drayson’s Surveying, and
married the Australian merchant.
Since then I have seen the pair. He was a
tobacconist in Melbourne ; a general storekeeper
and butcher in Saint Tiberias, and is now a pot-
bel—l mean a respectable squatter and M.L.C. in
one of the colonies. Last week (review Moore
Park : haw-haw ; greatly pleased ; efficiency, equal
any troops in the world ; loyalty, royalty, cheers,
beers, sneers) I met Janey Jardine, now Mrs.
Honourable (within the colony) Beeranbeef.
1 did not want to renew her acquaintance;
officialdom brought us within speaking distance,
and this is what she said: “Philomenostatos
Scott, don’t you remember the Zenith?”
I did not want to be rude; her manner was
friendly ; but the old wound gave my spleen a
turn, and seeing that her corsets were about GO,
what could I say but “Apparently, Mrs. Beeran-
beef, you have now found the latitude.”
Still, those old landings at Colintraive
and Ard-na-murchan can’t be forgotten; and
I am a welcome guest at the house of the Hon.
(within the colony) Beeranbeef, who is ten years
older than I. How shall it end ? I begin again
to love Janey and her ten thousand and the
agglomerated fortune of Beeranbeef !
Weep not for the dead, neither bemoan him;
but weep rather for him that goeth into a far
country.
Beeranbeef has gout. I am tolerably healthy.
How shall it end ? A great question. How shall
it end ?
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
“Being Irish, they never insulted women”
—such is the eulogy of the Kelly Gang, in one of
the last London papers, evidently from the hand
of a young man from Dublin—or Jimmy Hogan.
Reminds you of Robin Hood’s “I never robbed a
poor man yet.”
An American scientist lias, after much
experiment, succeeded in depriving a noisy dog of
the power to bark. The tyke still goes through
all the motions —wags its tail and opens his mouth
but no sound comes forth. The Bulletin joyfully
anticipates the application of this treatment to
the War Cry squawkers of the Harmy.

The Age seems to be incurably stupid in
its royal obituary notices. A cablegram announc-
ing the death of the Ozar’s uncle, in his 65th year,
was followed by a compound of extracts from
ancient and modern biographies, which repre-
sented the defunct Grand Duke as having “once
ventured to arrest his elder brother , the present
Czar of Russia,” and then concluded a hopeless
entanglement of references to “late emperor,”
“ new emperor,” and “the emperor,” with a plain,
comprehensible statement that “ in 1890 the uncle
of the Ozar was suddenly afflicted with the loss of
his reason,” No wonder. A man who started
life (in the Age) as the younger brother of his
nephew, would naturally go off his chump
sooner or later. There’s no fathoming the
mystery of royal history writing in these
“great” responsible newspapers. Here, perhaps,
is a case of tacking a piece of comparatively recent
intelligence to a yarn that had been set-up in the
Age office, ready against emergency, since a date
anterior to the nitro-glycerineing of poor Czar
Alexander II. But the recent mistake in describ-
ing Her Gracious Maj. as the Duchess of Teck’s
sister is not to be accounted for on a similar
hypothesis. One of David Syme’s gifted staff
must have manufactured that relationship out of
his own fat head, and we hope nothing of the sort
will occur again. There are now so many princesses
and grand-dukes and things that the Age can’t
sort ’em out.
One of the best-known characters in the
N.S.W. northern district, and especially about
Merriwa, is “Billy Munro.” The genial Billy
was, and still is, in the habit of now and then
running a score up for liquor supplied at the
various local “ Grands ” and other pubs, with less
pretentious names. After a round of drinks had
been supplied, he would exclaim : “Put that down
to Billy Munro.” It became a sort of second
nature with the shearers and locals around
Merriwa to hear “ Billy’s ” brief exclamation; so
that, in course of time, when “Billy,” had
settled up and departed on a cruise to some
other part of the country, a local, on ordering
drinks, would exclaim, with a sigh of satisfaction :
“Put that down to Billy Munro.” Billy’s pet
phrase at last became as popular and natural as
“ What’ll yer ’ave ?” From the rouseabout to the
squatter they all said “Put ’em down,” &c.
During the old chap’s absence —a couple of years—
a new and handsome Hebe had arrived to superin-
tend the mixing of gin and peppermint. To her
the inimitable “Billy” was a wild, hilarious
myth. For instance, when Mr. Blank, a farmer,
called in for a beer, he would say “ Put that down
to Billy Munro.” Of course Hebe would
smile and put it down to Mr. Blank. However,
one fine day the old, original William turned
up. He dropped into the bar where the “ new
gal ” was and called for a long beer ; after he had
demolished it he rattled off the old observation
“ put that down to Billy Munro.” The charmer
behind the counter laughed. “ Yes,” she said,
“ but to whom shall I put ’em down ? ” “ Why !”
roared Billy, “ to me, to Billy Munro." “Yes,”
said Hebe, “ they all say that when they get
drinks; but there’s no such person, is there?”
“Isn’t there,” yelled our hero —“who told yer
that, young ’ooman ? ” The old chap couldn’t
convince her until an old beer-chewcr, who was
hanging round, identified “Billy” in true Rip
Van Winkle style. William raved a-while, but
was at last pacified when told that life without the
use of Billy’s name, in that part at least, was like
a London Journal story without the black-haired
villain.

WOMAN'S RIGHTS & DAYS OF CHIVALRY
That strange growth of creation, the strong-
minded female, continues to pine for an extension
of her rights, political and otherwise ; and sighs
because what she calls “the days of chivalry”
are past. Well, perhaps they are, and it’s a good
job, too ! The phrase sounds very nice, but boil it
down to stern realism, and what does it mean?
It means an era when galvanised iron cavaliers
rushed at each other with sharpened clothes-props
for the honor of some “ ladye fayre.” Those '
times were not without their little drawbacks.
There were hardly any baths then, and the
“ladyes” mostly retired with their clothes on.
A quart of water was considered quite enough
for five girls to wash with, excepting in winter
time when they generally satisfied themselves
with a dry polish. It was the correct thing also
at dinner parties for a lady and gentleman to eat
off the same plate, and the one who could exhibit
the quickest despatch came in for the best share,
and reached for the other’s last piece of fat.
There were no tram-cars in those days, and con-
sequently no chance for the stout lady with a
plethoric handbag, a wet umbrella and innumer-
able parcels to crush herself and impediments into
an already overcrowded smoking-compartment
and look unutterable things at the unhappy male
creatures, who neglected, immediately her
presence blocked the doorway, to extinguish their
smoking idols, and bow her to half a dozen seats.
A return to the “ ancyent dayes ” would spoil all
this, and would, besides, deprive mankind of many
blessings already enjoyed. Foremost amongst
these is that greatest of all modern discoveries St.
Jacobs oil, the most perfect and reliable pain
conqueror ever introduced to the public. Its con-
venience for application, and the subtle power it
exercises over rheumatism, chest complaints and
every form of muscular disease, render it a valuable
family specific. There never was or will be a
remedy which acts with such sureness, rapidity
and certainty in relieving suffering.

UP TO DATE.
The Waterbury Watches are meeting with
genuine approval on all sides. They sell upon
their merits. A discerning public has not been
slow to recognise that they are accurate,
convenient, stylish in appearance, carefully con-
structed, and with absolutely interchangeable
parts. Their nominal price, too, is by no means
an unimportant feature. In the series for ladies
great improvements are to be noted, and these are
now turned out of a pattern and finish that cannot
fail to please the most fastidious. A word of
caution, however, is necessary. Don’t be misled
into buying inferior imitations of the Waterbury,
manufactured in Germany or Switzerland, and not
infrequently exhibited for sale ensconced in
Waterbury silk-lined boxes the better to deceive
the unwary purchaser. Intending purchasers of
the genuine articles should note that every watch
manufactured by the Waterbury Watch Co. has
one or both of the following distinctive marks on
the dial, viz., the word “WATERBURY” (in
fine type) and the interwoven letters “W. W.
Co.,” without which no watch sold as Waterbury
is genuine.

Brady’s Luck.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It became quite a by-word on the Adelaide Lead,
for Brady’s luck was so glaringly bad that, as his
friend and late partner, Doodle-doo Henry, put
it, “ It was worse than no luck at all.”
Brady himself was a well-known character in
and around Ballarat. He had been in his time
digger, cook, station-hand, mailman, stockman,
everything in turn and nothing long.' But during
all the time of his various and varied experiences,
Ballarat had always been a kind of head-centre
with him, to which he repaired whenever oppor-
tunity and the state of his funds permitted. When
there he always took a short spell at digging, but
his luck not taking any encouraging change for the
better, he generally threw up pick and shovel after
a few weeks or months of tentative labour, and
returned to other scenes and more promising
pursuits.
At last, however, after some years of knocking
about Victoria and N.S.W. he returned to Ballarat
for good and all, trying his luck with the other
diggers in the gullies and flats around. After a
while he took up his residence in what w r as then
known as the Adelaide Lead, and from the pecu-
liarities of his manners and appearance, as well as
in consequence of the phenomenally bad luck that
persistently followed him, soon became an
“identity” there and throughout the Ballarat
district generally. As to what he called his
“luck,” he had a very plausible theory which
he would explain in the following original way :
“You see, I was born with a caul, and everyone
knows as thg,t means luck. My mother—as has
been dead and buried these 40 years, a knowin’
old soul she was—she always said to me, ‘ Take
care of yourself, Judd, for you was born lucky,
and lucky you’ll be afore you die ’ —and if she
didn’t know the rights of it, I reckon nobody
don’t. All the doctors’ll - tell you it’s a lucky thing
to be born with a caul—it’s a regular medicil fact,
you’ll find it in them doctors’ books plain enough.
‘Takqcareof that caul, Judd,’ says ray mother
afore she went, ‘ never you part with it, for it’ll
bring you luck some day ; but if you lose it, then
it's all up with you.’ And I’ve taken jolly good
care to stick to it. I’ve worn it round my neck
night and day in this bit of a shammy bag, here
under my shirt, and I couldn’t part with it for
nothing. It’s bound to bring me luck some day,
and I reckon that day can’t be far off now. You
see, I’m 52, and I haven’t had any luck to speak of
yet, so it’s a moral the change will have to coirfe
soon. If it don’t,” he continued, a little less con-
fidently, “it’ll be too late to do me much good.”
It was in consequence of these representations
that Brady had been from time to time taken on
as a mate by several of the diggers on the lead.
Sentiment was most certainly not in vogue in that
boisterous life of the goldfields, nor did super-
stitious feeling exercise any appreciable influence
on the diggers, but in the minds of the few who
extended their friendship to the ancient there
was the thought that “ there was no harm in
giving the old geyser a show, and seoin’ if there
was anything in his bloomin’ luck.”
But in every case his co-operation was found to
bring anything but luck, and he was speedily
dropped. His best days were long past; his
strength and endurance were hot what they had
been, and he was found incapable of doing a hard
day’s work. First one tried him and gave him
hp, then another, and so it went on until he had
passed from man to man, and at length found
himself deserted by all, and looked upon —in con-
junction with his “luck”—with ridicule and
derision.
But Brady, in his way, was a philosopher, and
never lost faith in himself and his luck. His last
mate was a man known as Doodle-doo Henry—so
called from his invariable greeting of “ How do
you doodle-do ?” In parting with his mate, this
worthy had given utterance to the expression that
“Brady’s luck was worse than no luck at all,” and
this Brady himself had taken up with unreason-
able warmth.
“Of course, you know all about it,” he replied,
scornfully. “ A cove like you knows everything,
doesn’t he? A man would think you’d been
eddicated at college, by the flash way you talk.
But you ain’t read medical doctors’ books for all
that, or you’d know that a caul always brings
luck sooner or later. There ain’t nothin’ surer
than that. I’m not saying as you’re wrong in
wanting to work your claim by yourself. That’s
as how you Aggers it out to yourself. But you
young fellers have never got any head on you.
You’re too flash and snorty altogether. Why !by
waiting another few months —or maybe, even
days—there’s no telling what mightn’t have
turned up for you and me. But I ain’t complain-
ing,” he continued, a little wistfully, slowly
shouldering pick and shovel and turning away.
“There’s others as knows what’s what better than
you, and I won’t be long before I have another
mate, I know.”
His belief in himself and his luck was almost
pathetic, so entire and unqualified was it; but
unfortunately after the experience of Doodle-
doo Henry and the others, the rest of the diggers
on the Adelaide Lead did not view the matter in
the same light at all. They all refused his proffers
of partnership, and for a longtime the poor fellow
wandered round the different camps, solitary and
neglected. _
But he succeeded at last in picking up a mate,
and returning to the Lead with him in tow, soon
regained his customary cheerfulness.
Brady’s new mate was almost a boy, and, as
was soon made apparent, a new-chum. There
was no mystery about him at all, for he was open
enough as to his antecedents. By his own con-
fession he was a sailor and had served two voyages
from England to Australia before the mast. But
tiring of the sea and wishing to try his luck on
the diggings, he had left his ship and tramped it
from Melbourne to Ballarat. He gave his name
as Jim, quickly made friends with the men, and
set to work with very little loss of time.
Being asked what had prompted him to join an
old man like Brady, he answered quite good-
temperedly, “ Well, you see, being a new hand at
it, I thought it’d be best to get a messmate as
knew the ropes, so’s to put me in the way a bit
till I could get shipshape. You see, lads, digging
for gold’s different from working afore the mast,
and I’m out of my reckoning altogether. Besides,
my mate showed me a caul he’s got, and sailors
always take that as a lucky thing. So not having
anything better to lay to, I signed articles with
old Brady here, and we’ve agreed to work together
till something good turns up, which I hope won’t
be long a-lcming.”
He was such a hearty young fellow and had
' such vague ideas as to mining, that to the prac-
tical workers on the Adelaide Lead it seemed like
flying in the face of Providence that so much good
material should be thrown away on old Brady,
and several offers of shares in claims were made
to him. But he refused them all with a laugh and
a jest, electing to stick by his first choice.
“A fine feller I’d be,” he said, “ desarting a
shipmate afore the first week or two. ."ailors
ain’t built that way, it ain’t their natur. I won’t
go back on the old fellow-not so long as he
treats me fair.”
On his part, Brady evinced great admiration
and liking for his newly-found mate, and was
never tired of expatiating on his many good
qualities of head and heart. ~
“He’s a fine fellow is young Jim,” he would
say to anyone who would listen to him. “Sailors
are mighty taking in their ways, and young Jim’s
one of the best I’ve come across. The sea’s a
great eddicater, boys-it kind of opens a cove’s
eyes. That young Jim he’s got mighty good
ideas in his head, for all he’s a youngster. Oh,
him and me get on first-rate. We’re down 20
feet with the shaft, and things look pretty
promisin’. I guess the luck’s goin’ to change now.
I knew it was hound to come sooner or later, and
I’m glad I’ve got a mate like young Jim. It’ll
make both our fortunes, and, by the Hokey ! I’ll
make a man of him. He’s just the right sort for
me ; I wouldn’t ask for a better mate.”
But alas for poor old Brady and his luck!
Somehow the change did not come. They
bottomed their claim and found—to use a digging
phrase—they had been “sinking a grummet.”
Disheartened but not beaten, they pegged out
another claim, worked at it energetically for a
month or so, got a few pennyweights of gold out
of it and then gave it up in despair and tried
another. The storekeeper on the Lead began to
look sour when young Jim entered his bark
emporium for his weekly rations. There had been
no monev forthcoming for some time, and the
account Was running up. The luck had most
certainly not come yet, and every day it was
wanted more and more. Young Jim’s laugh was
heard less and less frequently ; he hardly ever
whistled now—and before it had been so shrill and
loud. Even old Brady—used to reverses as he
had been all through bis arduous life—seemed to
feel the position more keenly than formerly. His
simple faith in the luck that was bound to come
never for an instant faltered, but otherwise he
grew melancholy, and at times irritable.
During their fei» months of intimacy, an almost
fatherly affection for his young mate seemed to
have grown up in his heart, and it was as much on
the young man’s account as on his own, that he
secretly bewailed the unkind fate that stood
between him and his “luck.”
“ What’ll he think of me at all ?” he often said
to himself; “and me making on as we’d be sure
to have a streak of luck. He’ll be jacking up on
it, sure as death. But it’ll come if he’ll only
wait. There ain’t nothing surer nor that. But
it’s a main long time—a main long time, and it’s
weary waiting for a young feller.”
Poor old Brady ! It was weary waiting for him
too. Such weary waiting, that suddenly his hope-
ful spirit gave way, and with it health and
strength and energy. The immediate physical
cause of his sudden break-up was a cold contracted
by standing in their wet claim, and neglected
with the usual miner’s disregard of all the most
ordinary laws of health. The poor old fellow
took to his bunk, and never rose from it. He
gradually sank, growing each day weaker and
nearer his end. It was a complete break-up of
the system, and the doctor from Ballarat, sum-
moned hastily by the young sailor, gave little
hope from the first.
“ It’s all up with old Brady this time,” the old
man said one evening, “and I ain’t sorry I've
struck the right lead at last. I’ve been knockin’
round these here colonies ’till it’s nigh time I had
a rest of some sort. And I’m going where I’ll
have a good long one, if them parsons speak true.
But, look you here, my lad ; I'm sorry to go and
leave you. I am, true. I reg’larly collared to
you from the first, and I ses to myself directly I
cast eyes on you, ‘ That’s the cove as I’d like to
whack my luck with.’ 1 did, on my word. And
you’ve been a good mate to me, and a kind one,
too. You're looking arter me now kind and
thoughtful-like, and sitting with me here ’sted of
going and joining the other young fellers at the
public. I knew I’d got the right sort of a mate
when I’d got you.”
“You’re paying out too much rope, mate,”
answered the young sailor. “I ain’t no great
chalks. Avast a bit, and see if you can’t tackle a
morsel of grub.”
“No, I couldn’t, Jim,” he answered, seriously.
“ I don’t think I’ll be on the eat again— not hearty
like. But, see you here. I want to give you this.
I’ve worn it round my neck, man and boy, these
fifty years and more. It ain’t brought much luck
to me—not yet, but it’ll bring it to you instead,
which’ll be all the same. Take it, Jim, and keep
it. It’s too late now for it to do me much good,
but you’ll get it sure.”
The young sailor took it, half credulously, half
to please the invalid, and Brady, with a satisfied
sigh, soon fell off into a broken slumber.
He slept on and off during the night and the
greater part of next day, but his slumber was dis-
turbed and restless, and he awoke weaker than
before. During the evening his young mate left
him and hurried down to the public-house, where
most of the men on the Lead were accustomed to
assemble. Then he told them of the old man’s
plight, and so urgently represented his want of
money to buy the ordinary necessaries for the sick
man, that the minds of ids heareis were moved,
and a collection was made then and there and
handed to young Jim.
As Doodle-doo. Henry said, with a regretful
oath, “ There wasn’t any harm in old Brady. He
was straight enough only for that blessed maggot
he’d got in his head about his luck; so that now
he was down on his luck, poor old devil, it was
time for the boys to chip in and help a bit.”
With part of" the money given him, Jim bought
a few things for the invalid, and returned to his
post by his bunk. When Brady woke up, his
eyes brightened at sight of the young man.
“Are you there still, Jim?” he said, weakly.
“Well, now, that’s good of you. There ain’t
many would have stopped Dy a old Derwenter
like me. What’s that you’ve got? A bottle of
wine for me as the boys sent, thinking it might
do mo good ? Well, now ; I do call that friendly.
They’ve often made fun of old Brady, but I knew
their hearts was in the right place. But it ain’t
wine as’ll do me any good, Jim. I’ve sent my last
pick home. I only want a rest now—a good, long
rest. See here, Jim, my boy ; when I’m stiff I’d
like you to plant me away from the camp—some-
where in the bush where no one won’t disturb me.
You know that there big flowering gum down the
gully? Put me somewhere there, Jim, will you?
I’d like to be all by myself, shaded-like.”
The next day poor Brady breathed his last.
Almost his last words were, “Don’t lose the caul,
Jim; it’ll bring you luck as sure as death.” Then
with a half-remembered prayer on his lips he
quietly died.
- “ Poor old beggar !” said Doodle-doo Henry,
coming up half-an-hour afterwards to see how the
patient was progressing, “I’m sorry he’s gone.
There wasn’t any harm in him, but, by ginger 1
there was more kinds of fools mixed up in him
than in any other man in Ballarat. Say, Jim;

new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and

of an American bar, where all the strange, curious

can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among

I’ll stop and give you a hand to bury him, now
I’m here.”
“ No, I won’t do it to-night,” said Jim. “He
asked me to lay him along the gully there, where
hfe’d be quiet. It won’t take me long to knock
out a only it’s the gittin’of him there.' If
you’ll lexm me your horse and cart to-morrow, I’ll
manage the rest by myself.”
“Right you are,” answered the other, “You
can get ’em whenever you wantand having
done his duty, according to his lights, Doodle-
doo Henry went off with an exhilarated feeling
of having acted with quite exceptional ChrVitian
kindness.
Early next-morning the young sailor set about
the performance of his last service for hi# mate.
By the aid of Doodle-doo Henry’s wall-eyed
horse, he conveyed the body, covered decently by
a cloth, to the spot down the gully which Brady
had indicated. A large gum with drooping
fronds grew in the very centre of the miniature
defile, shading it and the low slopes that rounded
away on either sides. It was a pretty, grassy
spot, quiet and retired, where the sound of labour
on the Lead hardly pentrated.
Unharnessing the quiet horse, Jim allowed it
to feed about in the gully, and prepared for the
task of digging the grave. He cast one look at
the motionless form on the dray, and then set to
work with pick and shovel. He worked on for
about half-an-hour until he had dug a hole two or
three feet deep. Then he stopped, lifted himself
out, and made a step towards the dray.
“ No,” he muttered, “ I’ll bury him decent.
I’ll go down another foot or so.”
He jumped into the hole again, and commenced
using his pick vigorously. Three—four strokes
sounded with a hollow thud, and then all at once
a muttered exclamation broke from him; “By
George ! she’s stuck.” He was so wholly inex-
perienced, he had no idea what it was. He tried
to extricate the pick, but in vain; the point
seemed to be firmly imbedded. Then he took his
shovel and began digging the clay out round the
buried pick. A minute later a second cry rose to
his lips—but this time shrill, broken, and trium-
phant. He worked on, shovelling away the
sticky clay, with straining eagerness, and then
seizing hold of the handle of the buried pick, with
a mighty effort, tore it from the ground. Then
thrusting it up to its head in the loosened clay,
he used it as a lever. A huge mass, clay-covered
and yellow, was forced slowly upwards.
“ My God !” the young man cried, hoarsely,
“ I’ve got it at last. It’s a solid nugget.”
Discoloured and clay-covered as it was, there
was no mistaking it.
It was virgin gold.
Brady’s Luck had come at last.
DAVID G. FALK.
“Vesper.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The fringed blinds flap gently in the
evening breeze, which steals through the aperture
of the raised window. Fearful that the sound,
though it is soft as the murmur passing through
lovers’ lips to lure their lovers’, may disturb the
patient, the snowy-capped nurse moves to fasten
the drapery. She touches it lightly, and the
blind, being but lately hung on its window,
anspors to the spring and flies with a rustle to
coil itself around its roller. Vexed with herself for
what she considers her carelessness, the nurse,
while reaching her hand to the tassel to pull
down the blini once more, turns her eyes towards
the feed to see if the sick man has been awakened.
He needs sleep so much; sleep, if he is to be
saved from death, will be his salvation; and—
ah ! the noise has awoke him! He makes with
his head that half-turn on his pillow which is all
the movement his strength —his weakness, rather
—will permit. And his eyes stare pitifully from
the sockets into whose hollowness melt a thousand
fine wrinkles, each one the impress of a separate
pang. Six weeks before those wrinkles had no
place on the full, rounded cheeks that curved
only in laughter or smiles. And now the contour
has subsided into sharp angularities, and the flesh
has been burnt away underneath the skin which
hangs so loosely on the bones. Six weeks ago he
was a man for whom women might hunger—and
now? So gaunt and hideous a thing is he that he
dreads, in his occasional lapses into consciousness,
that he is repellant even to the “sister” of the
ward. But the good nurse sees only the patient,
and not the man. There is no sex in sickness.
“ Nurse !” The lips shape though they cannot
be said to utter the syllable.
She pulls the blind to the sill, and hastens to
his side, stooping over him to hear his words. He
moves his head again in a fashion that would be
deemed to be petulant Were he in health, and
makes her understand that he wishes the blind to
remain up, and not down. She returns to the
window, and eases the cord so that the tassel
presses noiselessly against, and rebounds from the
upper frame. And she steps aside, so that he
shall gaze out into the realm of the falling night.
She would raise him on his pillow, but she dare
not. So near is he to the Verge, the movement
might push him over.
The distressed brain that is as yet not fully
certain that the thing it sees is of this nether
world, and not an appanage of some other sphere,
strives valiantly to settle the doubt by endeavour-
ing to identify the mountain peak that thrusts
itself sombrely into the gold of the western sky.
But the forehead puckers yet more numerously as
the name eludes the weakened memory, and the
lips seek an utterance which will not come.
Then the glory of the sun-setting passes sud-
denly, and the ruddiness and the gold, and the
streaks of amethyst, and the puff-balls of fleecy
cloudlets that seem to have wandered from
. . . The place where white dreams dwell
And wreathe an unseen shrine,
lose their splendour in a moment, for over their
magnificence drops a purple shadow. It isasthough
some artist, of a genius so lofty as to make him
worthy to emblazon the walls of heaven itself, had
wrought a work too marvellous for human sight
to bear, and, in sudden contempt for the poor
mortal intelligence, drapes his great achievement
with a velvet pall. As the light goes out of the
sky, and the black peak merges its outline in the
mass of purple, a chill enters the room. The
nurse feels it, and instantly," fearing fbr her
charge, lowers the under-sash which had been
raised.
The top-frarneof the sash had stretched a black bar
across the upper pane. The window lowered, the
bar disappears, and
A sorrowing cry which was not all sorrow, but
held a note of joy, like that which rings a cadence
through a new-rnother’s agony, startles the house.
“Vesper! O Vesper ! My darling— come ! ”
He had risen in hia bed, and held out his arms
toward the west. There, gleaming lucidly above
the point of deepest darkness which marked the
mountain peak, shone the Evening Star. Virginal
in the beauty which bore witness to the death of
the day, she flashed into the room —and carried
him thence.
# * #
The door of the ward is opened carefully.
It is the night-nurse come to relieve the day-one.
But the latter will not go. He had called her
“ mercenary,” had the man who now lay dead,
when the fever had begun to work fiercely upon
him. , “Begone!” he had exclaimed; “I will
have no mercenary hands to touch me —give me
those sanctified by love, or none at all ! ’ And
then, in the next breath, he would tell her he was
but a wastrel, and his people had cast him off, and
it had come to this that in the battle for life he
shouldbave no ally* but the hired nurse. Mer-
cenary, in that she rendered the offices of tender-
ness for money, she was ; but not otherwise. Else
she had not given her hard-won evening leisure to
preparing him for his coffin.
“ If you do not mind, nurse !” said the “ day-
duty ” to the “ night-relief,” “ I will lay him out.”
And the “night-relief ” was but too willing, for
the final service to the dead-clay was repugnant to
her.
“ Have you reported the death ? ” she asks.
“ No—not yet ! ” replies the other. “ Will you
do it for me ? ”
The “ night-relief ” takes the patient’s card from
the mantel (it is a private ward, and therefore
the card is not suspended at the head of the bed)
and passes to the door, to inform the matron and
the doctor. The matron, however, meets her in
the cdrridor. She carries a paper in her hand.
“ Haw is private ward’s temperature to-night?
I have a telegram for him, but do not think I
shall give it to him.”
“ A telegram—oh ! He’s—gone !”
“Ah!. Poor girl!” and the matron hurries to
the room.
A candle has been lit, and the flame flickers in
the draught of her entrance. A beam falls
aslant on his face, and as the cheeks are already
witnessing that resurrection of colour which so
often lights the pyre of death with the fire of
youth, the three women fancy, for the moment,
he is not dead but sleeping, and smiling in his
sleep. For the moment only.
Then the matron reads out to the nurses, who
had seen many deaths, but were touched by this
one peculiarly because of the life that was now
speeding on its way to the chamber, the tele-
gram—:
“ Arrived,- beloved. Fast as love can bring me,
I come.- —Vesper.”
And when the day-nurse has clad him in his
cere-clothes, she places between the crossed
palms the bit of official paper. And the regula-
tions of the institution are violated, for the
hospital-morgue will not receive its tenant this
night. And the night-nurses steal out in the mid-
night and strip the Marguerite bushes of their
snow-stars, and weave in the still hours a tinj
cross and a great wreath. And Vesper comes,
with the dawn, with the dust of long travel upon
her garments, but in her eyes the quenchless light
of a fathomless love; Quenchless ! But dimmed
then, and while life endures.
PRICE WARUNG.
“A First Family.”
I.—Burke’s “Colonial Gentry,” Yol. 1.
The De Blankes, of Potts Point, Sydney, and
Wurramurradurra Downs, Lachlan District,
N.S.W., are a cadet-branch of the family of
which Lord De. Blanke, Baron Gonbroke, of
Diddleum Hall, shire, England, is head. (Vide
Burke’s Peerage ). The founder of the colonial
branch, Gavan De Blanke, was one of the earliest
settlers, &c., &c., &,c. . .
IL—The Bulletin’s “Burke,” Yol. 1 (Revised
Edition. )
The trial of Gavan De Blanke took place at the
Old Bailey Sessions on 9th Dec., 178-.
He was indicted for feloniously assaulting John
Pemberton Hey wood, Esq., on the King’s High-
way, on the 13th day of July last, in the parish
of Finchley, and putting him in bodily fear and
danger of his life; and feloniously taking vrom
his person, and against his will, a silk purse,
value Is. ; a base metal watch, value 405.; steel
chain, value Is. ; four cornelian seals, set in gold,
value <£B; a real morocco-leather tweezer case,
value Id. ; six guineas ; a crown piece and 9s. ;
and piece of foreign, copper coin, value one-
farthing, his property.
On Finchley Common, two men, mounted,
stopped prosecutor’s ehair and presented pistols.
Prosecutor declared Ire knew the gentlemen, and
that the lower part of the face of one, clear of the
crape, was like Gavan, as he had met him
several times at a friend’s in York. Prosecutor
told a friend : “If I was not sure that Gavan
de Blanke was not out of the Kingdom
(as he then supposed), I should be sure it was
him,” adding “he has a pretty strong Irish
brogue. I thought him a very agreeable young
man.” Had not seen him since robbery till he
saw him at Bow-street.
The question was one of identity. Mr. Garrow,
accused’s pleader, was asked by the Court if he
had any witnesses, and replied “No.” The
Court: “ Would the prisoner say anything?” Mr.
Garrow: “No, my Lord, on this occasion.”
Verdict: “Not guilty.”
Prosecutor; “My Lord, Mr. De Blanke, the
prisoner at the bar, says he has taken a passage to
go in the fleet to Botany Bay, and has obtained an
appointment in it as purser, and desires to be
discharged immediately.”
The Court : “ Let him be discharged.”

Fair or Foul?
“ The head was severed from the trunk, the lower part
of which was covered with loose earth. There were,
however, no signs of foul play visible.”—Sydney T).T.
The body lay all stark and busted,
The head had rolled a yard away,
The axehead in the brain had rusted,
T.ie body half-concealed with clay ;
The thorax was plugged up with lead,
The copper knowed the cove was dead,
Vet, as he spat and scratched his head,
J’oliceman Brine, he up and said—
“ Oi wondher how he kum this way,
There ain’t no thraces av foul play.”
Gore gouts were scattered on the ground,
The hands were full of rags and hair,
The viscera were strewed around,
Chops, cuts, and gashes great were there,
The stomach full of arsenic,
Enough to make a regiment sick,
B side the corpse a blood-stained pick,
Across it lay a clotted stick,
Yet Brine said, as he turned away—
“ There ain’t no thraces av foul play.”
And then, with pain and anguish great,
Brine formulated his report,
lie ground his teeth and scratched his pate,
As every zealous copper ought;
Ho knowed the man was dead, he wrote,
He’d tuk ther body down by boat,
A hinquich next he wint to float,
And showed the bloody shirt and coat,
And to the Crowner grave did say—
“ There worn’t no thraces av foul play.”
Casterfcon and Hamilton (Western Vic.)
people are just boiling over at the discovery that
Milner M‘Master (“Viator” of the Melbourne
D.T. and now of the Age) in his novel “ Our
Pleasant Vices,” has satirised some of their
district notabilities and unnotabilities. M‘Master
was for a time editor of the Casterton News, a
one-horse sheet owned by E. E. Hughes, who
bought it from W. S. Targett, now of Zeehan
(Tas.), and recently M.P. for Hartley, N.S.W.
Hughes is a Wesleyan and a lay-preacher, and
M‘Master is an ex-student of Mursell’s Baptist
Training College who had got too “ broad ” even
for the Baptists. The collocation of ed. and
proprietor was thus just beautiful. M'Master
would persist in chucking vitriolic criticism at
every Wesleyan sermon preached within 20 miles
of Casterton, and as everybody thereabouts who
isn’t a horse-coper is one “of the people called
Methodists,” the atmosphere of the News office—
a lean-to attached to a livery-stable—was oftener
that of Hades than the other place. Consequently,
Mac. vamoosed—and has had his revenge upon
the district in his book. Those who know
the country can spot with ease every villain and
cad in the volume—which has quite the usual
allowance.
IT RECALLS THE BLIZZARD.
In March, 1888, the great blizzard in America
almost extinguished the city of New York. The
like of it was never before seen. The snow com-
pletely stopped all local traffic. Not a horse or a
wheel could move. All the telegraph lines lead-
ing into the city were prostrated, and for two or
three days the people ot Boston and New York
communicated with each other by way of London
by means of the Atlantic cables. Thus messages
intended for three hundred miles distant were
sent six thousand miles, crossing the ocean twice.
An incident which recalls this experience to the
writer’s mind happened a short time ago here in
England. Suppose we let the gentleman in-
terested tell his own story. He says: “I have
suffered more or less from indigestion and
dyspepsia all my life. I had a bad taste in the
mouth, pain after eating, a poor appetite, and
sour stomach. My tongue was coated, and my
mouth constantly tilled with a watery fluid. No
matter what I ate, however light, it disagreed
with me and gave me pain. I had fulness of the
chest, and pain at my side, with a miserable, low,
dull feeling. From time to time I consulted a
doctor who gave me medicines, but they did very
little good. The doctor said that the coaling of
my stomach was disordered, and the mucous
membrane was inflamed. In 1877 I received a
pamphlet from New York telling of a medicine
called Mother Sergei's Curative Syrup, and of the
extraordinary cures it had effected, so 1 procured
a supply, and after taking the first I felt better,
and by the time I had taken four bottles nine, 1
was completely cured, and have been in good
health, as regards the indigestion, ever since.
Having studied the practice of herbs for many
years, I was in the habit of treating people for
erysipelas and other complaints, and was fre-
quently consulted by people in the district, so
after my remarkable cure, I was so impressed
with the merits of Seigel’s Syrup, that I procured
a large supply of the medicine, and recommended
it to all who were suffering, and people came from
far and near for it. I may mention that on a
Sunday my house was beseiged by miners from
Coal Pit Heath, and others from a distance. On
every hand I heard nothing but the loudest praise
of it and of the cures it effected, and the fame of
this medicine was spread throughout the West of
England with no other advertising than one party
tefling another of the benefit they had derived
from this wonderful medicine. I wish every one
to know of this, and if by publishing this state-
ment it will help others who may bo suffering as I
was, it will afford me pleasure.”
The letter from which the foregoing is an
extract is signed Moses Godwin, Old Sodbury
(Sodbury), Glos., and is dated April 9th, 1891.
He is a farmer.
The reader will notice that while the head-
quarters for the sale of Mother Seigel’s Syrup is
universally known to be in London, by a strange
chance Mr. Godwin’s first information concerning
it came from America, three thousand miles away,
which recalls the incident of the American blizzard
above narrated, and also shows that the fame and
usefulness of this medicine extend to all civilised
countries.
Mr. Benjamin Edgerton, grocer and provision
dealer, Plat Lane, WhixalJ, Whitchurch, Salop,
says: “While living with Mr. Roberts, Fens
Woods Farm, I first began to feel a dull, heavy
weight at my side, and noticed a bad taste in the
mouth with foul stomach and uncomfortable feel-
ing after eating. I had no appetite, and when I
sat down to the table I could not touch the food.
I had a good deal of pain and noise in the head,
and could not sleep for it. I was not fit for heavy
work, mid could only do light jobs about the
farm. After cutting a hedge I would go quite
faint, and had to sit down, and felt so much
depressed I could have 1 cried. Having always
been such a strong man I took it badly to bo
reduced to such a weak state. I took all kinds of
physic and saw a doctor, but his medicine only
eased me for a bit, and then I was worse than
before. I went on in this way for over a year,
when a servant that came to live at Mr. Roberts
told me of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. She had heard a gentleman talking about
it in the railway carriage, and he praised it so
much that I thought I would try it. After I had
taken two bottles fny food did me good, and I
gained strength, and by persevering with the
syrup I soon got as strong as ever, and have never
ailed anything since.”

Dear Bulletin, When is Sydney to have
her morning Protectionist paper? How much
longer are the Herald and Telegraph to be
allowed possession of the field with the
power to misrepresent and to suppress? The
Government are handicapped now in con-
sequence of this--and they, with the Protec-
tionist cause, will suffer keenly if a general
election comes along with these papers still
holding a monopoly of the morning news. What
is the Star proprietary up to ? Unless they have
some definite project in hand for bringing out a
morning paper, they are playing the dog in the
manger by refusing 1o publish any correspondence
relating to the establishment of such a paper.
Yours, &c., U. T.
The Cook town (Q.) hospital authorities
object to having incurable patients left on their
hands. A Chinaman past all remedy, who seemed
to belong to nobody, was among the inmates, so
the hospital people simply carried him out and
sat him down in the street. Sundry Christians
presently put him in a dray and took him to
Chinatown, but the yellow men there looked at
him with a hard, Lshy eye, and said “No savee.”
1 he B.M. then got two constables to carry him
back to the hospital, but the officials banged the
door upon them, 'i hen the Mayor sent a written
order for his admittance, and "the hospital once
more said no. Last of all, the law fell back on
its good, old remedy : it charged the dying cripple
with vagrancy, no visible means of support, ob-
structing a public thoroughfare, disorderly con-
duct and tearing somebody’s uniform, and now
poor John will be able to “ peg out” in limbo.
Some European fiend has patented a
contrivance by which bills can be pasted, without
the use of a ladder, to a height of fifty feet., We
trust he may be killed as a public enemy before
his horrid device reaches Australia. Every
Australian city is already plastered over with a
leprosy of bills—glaring red bills like flames stuck
on a fence, howling yellow bills like the great
plague nailed to a dead wall, blue bills, green
bills like the backs of deceased men that have gone
mouldy, murky black bills which remind one of
the shadow cast by a disused hearse, white bills,
pallid bills that seem to suffer from loss of blood,
and all sorts of bills. Moat of them arc torn to
fragments, and their remnants' hang loose and
flap disgustedly in the night air. The rain washes
the paint off them, and it drips with a melancholy
cadence and a slosh on the hat of the passer-by.
Then the bills themselves dropoff, and lie horribly
in the gutter, or fly on the wings of the southerly
buster and get into the s ye of the peaceful citizen ;
and now and then the bill-poster’s hoard ng falls
down bodily and flattens the stranger underneath.
This is how things are now, but with the advent
of the new bill-posting machine the hoarding will
grow to the height of a three-storey house, and
there will be six times as many bills on it as there
are at present, and men will come round at night
and stick hair-restorer and soap notices on the
chimneys where we can’t get at them to drag
them down, and will put slop-clothing “ads.” on
the roof, and life won’t be worth having. What
this country wants badly is a law to make it a
felony to put up a bill anywhere.
\ cars ago the chappies wore their hats on
the back of their heads —to conceal the surplus.
Now they pull them down where the forehead
should be to hide the deficiency.
You can obtain from B. Mountcastle & Sons, of
Albert-street, Brisbane, goods of a similar class to
that retailed by the leading West-end hosiers.
A very stupid cartoon in a Melbourne
weekly represented that worthy cricket-crank,
Lord Sheffield, as clearin',' but of An-lralia with a
big bag of profits, and the old gentleman was
stupid enough to feel hurt by the insinuation.
Nobody in the cricket world ima; dies that the
terms on which Lord S. lured his little picnic will
leave him with a surplus when the fun is finished,
or that he would pocket a surplus if it were there.
And as he can't be said to exist in any other
world the noble Lord needn't worry himself.
